Non-Muslims Not Sworn in Their Places of Worship
Ruling with added commentary on prohibition for Muslim to enter places of worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that non-Muslim litigants are not sworn in their places of worship and explain the reasoning based on the judge's prohibition from entering such places.

Core Idea
Non-Muslims are not sworn in their places of worship because the judge is prohibited from entering them.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in Hanafi qada (judicial procedure): administering oaths to non-Muslim litigants. The text establishes that the oath is not taken in the litigant's place of worship, because the judge is forbidden from entering churches and synagogues. This ruling connects to broader principles of respect for Islamic sacred spaces and the boundaries of judicial conduct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The passage begins with 'ولا يحلفون في بيوت عبادتهم' — non-Muslims are not sworn in their places of worship. State this as the primary ruling.
This is a procedural rule for qada.
2
Identify the Reason
The reason given is 'لأن القاضي لا يحضرها بل هو ممنوع عن ذلك' — the judge does not attend them, rather he is prohibited from doing so. Understand that the prohibition is on the judge, not on the litigant.
3
Understand the Basis of Prohibition
The commentator elaborates, citing al-Tatarkhaniyyah, that it is disliked (makruh) for any Muslim to enter a church or synagogue. The dislike is classified as makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked), as the text says 'الظاهر أنها تحريمية'.
The reason for dislike is that these are gatherings of devils, not because the Muslim has no right to enter.
4
Apply the Consequence
The commentator mentions issuing a fatwa of ta'zeer for a Muslim who habitually attends a church. This shows the seriousness of the prohibition.
Ta'zeer is a discretionary punishment, indicating the act is sinful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يحلفون في بيوت عبادتهم لأن القاضي لا يحضرها بل هو ممنوع عن ذلك؛ كذا في الهداية. ولو قال المسلم لا يحضرها لكان أولى لما في التتارخانية: يكره للمسلم الدخول في البيعة والكنيسة، وإنما يكره من حيث إنه مجمع الشياطين لا من حيث إنه ليس له حق الدخول، والظاهر أنها تحريمية لأنها المرادة عند إطلاقها، وقد أفتيت بتعزير مسلم لازم الكنيسة مع خالقه ا هـ.
And they are not sworn in their places of worship because the judge does not attend them, rather he is prohibited from that; thus in al-Hidayah. And if it was said 'the Muslim does not attend them' it would be more appropriate, because in al-Tatarkhaniyyah: it is disliked for a Muslim to enter the synagogue and church, and it is disliked because it is a gathering of devils, not because he has no right to enter; and the apparent [view] is that it is prohibitively disliked because that is what is meant when [the ruling] is unqualified; and I have issued a fatwa of ta'zeer for a Muslim who frequents a church, with its Creator, end.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It establishes that non-Muslim litigants are not to be sworn in their places of worship (churches, synagogues, etc.) because the judge is prohibited from entering such places. The author notes that the ruling is derived from al-Hidayah. The commentator adds that it would be even more appropriate to say that a Muslim judge should not attend, citing al-Tatarkhaniyyah that it is makruh for a Muslim to enter a church or synagogue. The dislike is because these places are considered gatherings of devils, not because the Muslim has no right of entry. The commentator clarifies that this disliking is at the level of tahrimi (prohibitive dislike), as the general usage of 'karahah' in fiqh often indicates prohibition. Finally, the commentator mentions having given a fatwa that a Muslim who habitually attends a church should be given ta'zeer (discretionary punishment).

Key Terms
بيعة (bi'a): A church or place of worship for Christians; synagogue may also be included.
كنيسة (kanisah): A church; place of worship for Christians.
تعزير (ta'zeer): Discretionary punishment for sins that do not have a fixed hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are non-Muslims not sworn in their places of worship according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because their places of worship are impure
B) Because the judge is prohibited from entering them
C) Because they do not believe in God
D) Because they prefer to swear elsewhere

Key Takeaway
Non-Muslims are not sworn in their places of worship because the judge is prohibited from entering them; the prohibition is makruh tahrimi, and habitual attendance may warrant ta'zeer.

Oaths in Litigation: Categorical vs. Denial of Knowledge
Distinguishing oath types with examples from Hanafi fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the categorical oath (yamin al-batat) and the oath on denial of knowledge (yamin nafi al-ilm), apply the correct type in given scenarios, and explain the exception for guarantee-based claims.

Core Idea
Swear categorically on your own act, but on denial of knowledge for another's act—unless you guaranteed the item.

In Hanafi judicial practice, the form of an oath depends on whose act is being sworn about. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the key distinction: a party swears categorically (e.g., 'I did not do it') for his own act, and on denial of knowledge (e.g., 'I do not know that he did it') for another's act. This is supported by the Prophet's method of swearing the Jews. However, Imam al-Hulwani notes an important exception: when a seller guaranteed an item free from defects, he swears categorically about the defect, even though it is the act of the item. This lesson will walk through the rule, the hadith proof, the exception, and numerous examples from Hanafi sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two oath types
Understand the difference between a categorical oath (al-batat) which is a decisive denial of an action ('I did not do it'), and an oath on denial of knowledge (nafi al-ilm) which denies knowing about a fact ('I do not know that he did it'). The type depends on whose action is being sworn about.
The key is whether the swearer is denying his own act or another's.
2
Study the dalil
The Prophet (ﷺ) made the Jews swear: 'By Allah, you did not kill him, nor do you know his killer.' This hadith demonstrates both types: 'ma qataltum' is a categorical denial of their own act, and 'wa la 'alimtum' is a denial of knowledge about another's act. This establishes the principle.
The hadith is the primary evidence for the distinction.
3
Apply the general rule
When a person swears about his own action, he must use the categorical form. When swearing about another's action, he uses denial of knowledge. This is the default rule.
4
Analyse the exception
Imam al-Hulwani noted that in claims of defect (return due to defect), the seller swears categorically even though the defect is an action of the slave. The reason is that the seller guaranteed the item free from defects; his oath relates to his own guarantee, not the slave's act. Similarly, a depositary who claims the owner took back the deposit swears categorically because he has direct knowledge.
The exception applies when the swearer has a personal guarantee or direct knowledge of the fact.
5
Work through the examples
Review the examples from the passage: executor vs. agent in defect claims, conditional divorce, and others. Note that if an oath is required on knowledge but the person swears categorically, it suffices, but not vice versa. Understand the practical application.
The correct use of oath forms affects the outcome of the case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المؤلف رحمه الله إلى أنه يحلف على نفي العلم في فعل الغير وعلى البتات في فعل نفسه ولهذا حلف عليه السلام اليهود بالله ما قتلتم ولا علمتم له قاتلاً قال الإمام الحلواني : هذا الأصل مستقيم في المسائل كلها إلا في الرد بالعيب فإن المشتري إذا ادعى الإباق ونحوه فإن البائع يحلف على البتات مع أنه فعل الغير لأن البائع ضمن له المبيع سالماً عن العيوب، فالتحليف يرجع إلى ما ضمن بنفسه فحلف على البتات ألا ترى أن المودع إذا قال إن الوديعة قبضها صاحبها يحلف على البتات وكذا الوكيل بالبيع إذا ادعى قبض الموكل الثمن فإنه يحلف على البتات لادعائه العلم بذلك ؛ كذا ذكر الشارح وفي الخلاصة لو قال إن لم يدخل فلان الدار اليوم فامرأته طالق ثم قال إنه دخل يحلفه على البتات بالله أنه دخل الدار اليوم اهـ. أنه فعل الغير لكونه ادعى علماً به. وفي القنية : باع الوصي عبداً فادعى المشتري به عيباً ولا بينة له يحلف الوصي على البتات والوكيل على العلم لأن العبد في يد الوصي فيعلم بالعيب ظاهراً بخلاف الوكيل .اهـ. ومما يحلف فيه على نفي العلم ما في القنية : ولو اشترى جارية من رجل فادعت امرأته أنها اشترتها قبل هذا ولا بينة فلها أن تحلف المشتري على العلم اهـ. ومنه ما فيها أيضاً : قال في حال مرضه ليس لي شيء من دار الدنيا ثم مات عن زوجة وبنت وورثة فللورثة أن يحلفوا زوجته وابنته على أنهما لا يعلمان شيئاً من تركة المتوفى بطريقه اهـ . وفي البزازية : في يده جارية يقول أودعنيها فلان الغائب وبرهن فقال المدعي باعها أو وهبها بعد الإيداع منك وأنكره المدعى عليه يحلف بالله ما باعها أو وهبها منك. في يده عبد ورثه أمه ادعى آخر أنه كان أودعه من أبيه يحلف على العلم .اهـ. ثم في كل موضع وجبت اليمين فيه على العلم فحلف على البتات كفى وسقطت عنه وعلى عكسه لا، ولا يقضى بنكوله عما ليس واجباً عليه والبتات بمعنى البت بمعنى القطع وكان اليمين على نفي العلم قطع فيها بخلاف الأخرى. وفي بعض كتب الفقه البت بدل البتات ولم أر فيما عندي من كتب اللغة أن البتات بمعنى القطع، وإنما ذكر في القاموس أن البت بمعنى القطع وأن البتات الزاد والجهاز ومتاع البيت والجمع أبتة ولم يذكره في المصباح والمغرب.
And the author, may Allah have mercy on him, indicated that he (the defendant) swears on denial of knowledge regarding the action of another, and on categorical negation regarding his own action. For this the Prophet, peace be upon him, made the Jews swear: 'By Allah, you did not kill him, nor do you know his killer.' Imam al-Hulwani said: This principle is sound in all cases except in the case of return due to defect; if the buyer claims that the slave ran away and the like, the seller swears categorically even though it is the act of another, because the seller guaranteed the sold item free from defects, so the oath reverts to what he guaranteed himself, so he swears categorically. Do you not see that the depositary, if he says the owner took the deposit, swears categorically; likewise the agent for sale, if he claims the principal received the price, swears categorically because he claims knowledge of that. This is what the commentator mentioned. In al-Khulasa: If he said, 'If so-and-so does not enter the house today, his wife is divorced,' then he said he did enter, the man makes him swear categorically, 'By Allah he entered the house today.' That is the act of another because he claimed knowledge of it. In al-Quniya: An executor sold a slave, and the buyer claimed a defect with no evidence; the executor swears categorically, while the agent swears on knowledge, because the slave was in the custody of the executor and he knows of the defect outwardly, unlike the agent. Included in swearing on denial of knowledge is what is in al-Quniya: If a man bought a slave girl from a man, and his wife claimed she bought her before this with no evidence, she may make the buyer swear on knowledge. Also in it: He said during his illness, 'I own nothing of the world,' then died leaving a wife, daughter, and heirs; the heirs may make his wife and daughter swear that they do not know of any estate of the deceased in his path. In al-Bazzaziyya: In his hand is a slave girl; he says so-and-so deposited her with me, and he proves it; the claimant says he sold or gave her after the deposit, and the defendant denies; he swears by Allah that he did not sell or give her to you. In his hand is a slave inherited from his mother; another claims that his father deposited it with him; he swears on knowledge. Then, whenever an oath is required on knowledge and he swears categorically, it suffices and is dropped from him, but not the opposite. Nor is judgment passed on his refusal (nukul) regarding what is not obligatory. Al-batat means cutting decisively. The oath on denial of knowledge cuts decisively, unlike the other. In some books of fiqh, 'bat' is used instead of 'batat'. I have not seen in the linguistic books that 'batat' means 'cutting'; rather, in al-Qamus, 'bat' means 'cutting' and 'batat' means provisions, equipment, and household goods, with plural 'abittah'. It is not mentioned in al-Misbah or al-Mughrib.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses the distinction between two types of oaths in litigation: the oath on denial of knowledge (yamin 'ala nafi al-ilm) and the categorical oath (yamin 'ala al-batat). The author, al-Marghinani, indicates that when a person is swearing about the action of another, he must swear on denial of knowledge (e.g., 'I do not know that he did it'), but when swearing about his own action, he must swear categorically (e.g., 'I did not do it'). The proof is the hadith where the Prophet (ﷺ) made the Jews swear: 'By Allah, you did not kill him, nor do you know his killer' – swearing both categorical and denial. Imam al-Hulwani, however, notes an exception: in the case of a claim for a defect in a purchased item (e.g., the buyer claims the slave ran away), the seller swears categorically even though the action is that of the slave, because the seller guaranteed the item free from defects. The commentary then provides numerous examples from Hanafi sources: the depositary swears categorically when claiming the owner took the deposit, because he has direct knowledge; the agent swears categorically when claiming the principal received the price; in conditional divorce cases, the husband swears categorically regarding entry; the executor swears categorically regarding defects because he has custody; while the ordinary agent swears on knowledge. If an oath is required on knowledge but one swears categorically, it is sufficient, but not vice versa. The passage also discusses the linguistic meaning of 'batat' and 'bat', noting that some fiqh books use 'bat' instead. This ruling is important for judges and litigants, ensuring the correct form of oath is administered based on the party's relationship to the fact.

Key Terms
البتات (al-batāt): A categorical, decisive denial; an oath that affirms absolute negation (e.g., 'I did not do it').
نفي العلم (nafi al-ilm): An oath denying knowledge of a fact (e.g., 'I do not know that he did it').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a claim for a defect in a purchased slave, who swears categorically?
A) The buyer
B) The seller
C) The witness
D) The judge

Key Takeaway
The correct form of oath—categorical or denial of knowledge—depends on whether the fact relates to the swearer's own act or another's, with an exception for guaranteed items or direct knowledge.

Ransoming the Oath: Ruling, Proof, and Conditions
Ruling on ransoming the oath with proof from sahaba and hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a defendant ransoming or settling his oath, identify the proof, and explain the conditions for its validity.

Core Idea
A defendant may ransom his oath to protect his honor, but only if the claimant is truthful.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a practical scenario in litigation: a defendant who is reluctant to swear an oath may pay the claimant to avoid it. The ruling validates this practice, citing the actions of companions and a prophetic hadith. However, the permission is conditional on the claimant's truthfulness, and not every financial transaction regarding the oath is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what action is being discussed (ransom or settlement of the oath) and the legal ruling (valid, defendant no longer swears).
The ruling applies to both iftida' (ransom) and sulh (settlement) regarding the oath.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited: actions of Caliph Uthman and Hudhayfa, and the hadith 'Protect your honors with your wealth.'
These are ahad (single) narrations, providing zanni thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith indicates permissibility: protecting honor includes avoiding the consequences of swearing (gossip, accusation). Ransoming the oath preserves honor.
The wisdom of avoiding false accusations underlies the ruling.
4
Note the conditions and invalid alternatives
Identify the key condition: the claimant must be truthful. Distinguish ransom/settlement from buying the oath (invalid) and from merely dropping the oath (invalid).
The condition ensures that the payment is a genuine substitute for the right, not a bribe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو افتدى المنكر يمينه أو صالحه منها على شيء صح ولم يحلف بعده . قوله : (ولو افتدى المنكر يمينه أو صالحه منها على شيء صح ولم يحلف بعده) أما الجواز فلما روي عن عثمان رضي الله عنه أنه ادعى عليه أربعون درهماً فأعطى شيئاً وافتدى بيمينه ولم يحلف . وعن حذيفة أنه افتدى بيمينه بمال ولأنه لو حلف يقع في القيل والقال فإن الناس بين مصدق ومكذب فإذا افتدى بيمينه فقد صان عرضه وهو حسن قال عليه السلام «ذبوا عن أعراضكم بأموالكم وذكر الصدر الشهيد أن الاحتراز عن اليمين الصادقة واجب اهـ. ومراده ثابت بدلیل جواز الحلف صادقاً وإنما لا يحلف بعده لأنه أسقط خصومته بأخذ البدل عنه. قيد بالفداء والصلح لأنه لو اشترى بيمينه لم يجز وكان له أن يستحلفه لأن الشراء عقد تمليك المال بالمال واليمين ليست بمال؛ كذا في النهاية وظاهر ما قرره الشارح أن أخذ المال الفداء والصلح عن اليمين إنما يحل إذا كان المدعي محقاً ليكون المأخوذ في حقه بدلاً كما في الصلح عن الإنكار، فلو كان مبطلاً لم يحل ، والضمير في «منها عائد إلى يمينه أي بدلها. وفي شرح مسكين : ثم الافتداء قد يكون بمال بمثل المدعي وقد يكون بأقل منه، وأما الصلح فإنما يكون منه على مال هو أقل من المدعي غالباً ؛ كذا في النهاية اهـ. قيد بالإسقاط ضمن الافتداء والصلح لأن إسقاطها قصداً غير صحيح لما في دعوى البزازية آخر الرابع عشر : قال المدعي برئت من الحلف أو تركت عليه الحلف أو وهبت لا يصح وله التحليف بخلاف البراءة عن المال لأن التحليف للحاكم اهـ. والله أعلم.
And if the defendant ransoms his oath or settles regarding it for something, it is valid and he is not sworn afterwards. His statement: (And if the defendant ransoms his oath or settles regarding it for something, it is valid and he is not sworn afterwards). As for permissibility, it is based on what is narrated about Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him: someone claimed forty dirhams against him, so he gave something and ransomed his oath and did not swear. And from Hudhayfa, that he ransomed his oath with property. And because if he swears, he falls into gossip; people are between believers and deniers. So if he ransoms his oath, he protects his honor, and that is good. The Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Protect your honors with your wealth.' And al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned that avoiding the truthful oath is obligatory. His intent is established by the permissibility of swearing truthfully, but he does not swear afterwards because he dropped his litigation by taking the substitute for it. It is restricted to ransom and settlement, because if he buys his oath, it is not valid, and the other party may still swear him, because purchase is a contract of exchange of property for property, and an oath is not property. This is in al-Nihaya. And apparent from what the commentator determined is that taking money as ransom or settlement for the oath is only permissible if the claimant is truthful, so that the taken money is a substitute in his right, as in settlement upon denial. If he were false, it is not permissible. The pronoun 'from it' refers to 'his oath', i.e., its substitute. In Sharh Miskin: Ransom may be with property equal to the claim or less. As for settlement, it is usually less than the claim. Restricted to dropping within ransom and settlement because intentionally dropping the oath is not valid. As in Da'wa al-Bazzaziyya end of the fourteenth: If the claimant says, 'I am free from swearing' or 'I leave swearing him' or 'I gift it,' it is not valid, and he may still swear him, unlike discharge from property, because swearing belongs to the judge. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the ruling on a defendant who ransoms his oath (iftada' al-yamin) or settles (sulh) with the claimant regarding the oath, instead of swearing. The ruling is that it is valid, and after paying, the defendant no longer has to swear. The proof is from the actions of Caliph Uthman and Hudhayfa (may Allah be pleased with them) who ransomed their oaths. The wisdom is to avoid public gossip and protect one's honor, and the hadith 'Protect your honors with your wealth' supports it. The commentator notes that this is only permissible if the claimant is truthful, similar to settlement upon denial. If the claimant is false, it is not permissible. The passage also distinguishes between ransom/settlement and buying an oath: buying the oath is invalid because an oath is not property. Additionally, merely dropping the right to swear (isqat) is not valid; the claimant still retains the right. This ruling is practical: a defendant may avoid swearing by paying, but it must be a genuine settlement, not a purchase.

Key Terms
الافتداء (al-iftidā'): Ransom: the defendant gives money to the claimant to be released from the obligation to swear.
الصلح (al-ṣulḥ): Settlement: the parties agree to drop the oath in exchange for a payment, often less than the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for the claimant to accept ransom for the oath?
A) The claimant must be truthful
B) The defendant must be wealthy
C) The claim must be for property
D) The oath must be obligatory

Key Takeaway
Ransoming the oath is valid only if the claimant is truthful, and it must be a genuine settlement, not a purchase or mere dropping of the right.

Rule on Mutual Oaths and the Effect of Nukul
Rule on mutual oaths and effect of refusal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mutual oaths when both parties have evidence and explain why refusal to swear (nukul) leads to judgment when the oath is obligatory.

Core Idea
Refusal to swear an obligatory oath results in judgment against the refuser.

This lesson delves into the chapter of mutual oaths (bab al-tahalf), specifically the scenario where both parties present evidence. The author explains that when the oath is obligatory, refusal (nukul) leads to a judgment against the refusing party. A potential problem arises because refusal might seem inconsequential, but the text resolves it by asserting that the obligatory nature makes the refusal a failure, thus justifying the judgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling Context
Recognize that the passage deals with mutual oaths (tahalf) in a dispute where both parties have evidence. The ruling applies when the defendant's oath is obligatory (e.g., after the plaintiff's evidence preponderates).
2
State the Ruling on Nukul
The ruling: If the party whose oath is obligatory refuses to swear (nukul), judgment is given against that party. The text states: 'ولو نكل يقضى عليه' (and if he refuses, he is judged against).
3
Analyze the Perceived Problem
A potential issue is raised: why should refusal lead to judgment when the oath is not binding in itself? The text acknowledges this: 'قيل هذا الفرع مشكل' (it was said this branch is problematic). The problem is that refusal does not constitute an admission of the claim.
4
Trace the Resolution
The resolution: The oath is obligatory (wajib) even though it may be dropped after refusal. Since it was obligatory, the refusal is considered a failure to fulfill a duty, so the judge can rule against the refuser. The text says: 'أنه مسقط لليمين الواجبة عليه فاعتبر' (it drops the obligatory oath, so it is considered).
5
Contrast with the Opposite Case
In the opposite scenario—where the oath is not obligatory—refusal has no effect. The refuser may swear again later. This distinction shows that the ruling hinges on the obligation of the oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب التحالف لما ذكر حكم يمين الواحد ذكر حكم يمين الاثنين إذا الاثنان عبد الواحد. والتحالف سقط عنه الحلف إذ البت أقوى ولو نكل يقضى عليه، وقيل هذا الفرع مشكل اهـ. أقول: وجه الإشكال أنه كيف يقضى عليه أنه لعدم لزومه له فلا يكون بذلاً مكلف إلى البت فنكوله عنه مع غير ولا إقراراً. ويزول الإشكال بأنه مسقط لليمين الواجبة عليه فاعتبر فيكون قضاء بعد نكول عن يمين مسقط للحلف عنه بخلاف عكسه، ولهذا يحلف ثانياً في صورة العكس لعدم سقوط الحلف عنه بها فنكوله عنه لعدم اعتباره والاجتزاء به فلا يقضى عليه بسببه تأمل . باب التحالف قوله : (وقيد بالاختلاف في القدر لأنهما لو اختلفا الخ) في نور العين عن قاضيخان : اختلف
Chapter of Mutual Oaths: When he mentioned the ruling of the oath of one person, he mentioned the ruling of the oath of two, when the two are [servants?] of one master. And mutual oath (tahalf) drops the oath from him because the categorical (al-bat) is stronger, and if he refuses (nukul), he is judged against. It was said: This branch is problematic. He said: The face of the problem is: How can judgment be passed against him? Because it is not binding on him, so it is not an exertion that obligates him to the categorical; his refusal is not an admission. The problem is removed by [saying] that it is a dropped oath (musqat) that is obligatory upon him, so it is considered; thus, it is a judgment after refusal of an oath that drops the oath from him, unlike the opposite. For this reason, he swears again in the opposite case because the oath is not dropped from him by it; his refusal is not considered and not sufficient, so no judgment is passed against him. Consider. Chapter of Mutual Oaths: His statement: (And it is restricted to disagreement in the price because if they differed...) in Nur al-Ayn from Qadikhan: they differed.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on mutual oaths (bab al-tahalf) discusses the scenario where two parties each have evidence in a dispute over the price or subject matter. The opening line states that the author has now moved from the oath of a single person to the oath of two persons, i.e., mutual oaths. The rule is that if both parties produce evidence, the one who proves the increase (e.g., the buyer claiming a higher price paid vs. seller claiming lower) wins. The commentary then discusses a problem: if the oath is stronger (categorical) and the defendant refuses to swear (nukul), judgment is given against him. But some find this problematic because the oath might not be obligatory. The resolution is that the oath is dropped (musqat) once refused, but since it was obligatory, the refusal constitutes a failure, leading to judgment. In the opposite case (if the oath is not obligatory), refusal has no effect. The text is incomplete, ending with a reference to Qadikhan.

Key Terms
التحالف (al-taḥāluf): Mutual oaths between two parties when each has evidence and they disagree.
النكول (al-nukūl): Refusal to take an oath in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling if a party refuses to swear an obligatory oath?
A) The case is dismissed.
B) The other party is asked to swear instead.
C) Judgment is given against the refuser.
D) The judge decides based on evidence only.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, refusing an obligatory oath (nukul) leads to judgment against the refuser.

Ruling on a Defendant Ransoming His Oath
Ruling and commentary on defendant paying to avoid oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a defendant ransoming his oath and identify the condition that makes it permissible.

Core Idea
A defendant may ransom his oath only if the claimant is truthful.

This lesson examines a unique Hanafi ruling: a defendant can pay the claimant to avoid swearing an oath. Based on prophetic hadith and precedent from the Companions, the ruling balances truthfulness and honor. However, there is a critical condition: the claimant must be truthful for the payment to be lawful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the defendant ransoming his oath (al-iftida'). The ruling is that it is permissible (jaiz/mubah) and he does not swear afterwards.
The text begins 'ولو افتدى المنكر يمينه أو صالحه منها على شيء صح'.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith 'Protect your honor with your wealth' and the actions of Uthman and Hudhayfa (may Allah be pleased with them).
The hadith is ahad (single-narrator), so its thubut is zanni.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The command 'protect' implies that it is permissible to use wealth to protect one's honor, and ransoming the oath is a form of protection.
The argument is that avoiding potentially harmful gossip is a legitimate goal.
4
Note the condition
The permission is conditional on the claimant being truthful. If the claimant is false (mubtil), taking money to avoid the oath is not halal.
The text states: 'إنما يحل إذا كان المدعي محقاً'.
5
Distinguish from buying the oath
Ransoming is valid, but buying the oath (as a sale) is not, because an oath is not property.
The text says: 'لو اشترى بيمينه لم يجز'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو افتدى المنكر يمينه أو صالحه منها على شيء صح ولم يحلف بعده . قوله : (ولو افتدى المنكر يمينه أو صالحه منها على شيء صح ولم يحلف بعده) أما الجواز فلما روي عن عثمان رضي الله عنه أنه ادعى عليه أربعون درهماً فأعطى شيئاً وافتدى بيمينه ولم يحلف . وعن حذيفة أنه افتدى بيمينه بمال ولأنه لو حلف يقع في القيل والقال فإن الناس بين مصدق ومكذب فإذا افتدى بيمينه فقد صان عرضه وهو حسن قال عليه السلام «ذبوا عن أعراضكم بأموالكم» وذكر الصدر الشهيد أن الاحتراز عن اليمين الصادقة واجب اهـ. ومراده ثابت بدليل جواز الحلف صادقاً وإنما لا يحلف بعده لأنه أسقط خصومته بأخذ البدل عنه. قيد بالفداء والصلح لأنه لو اشترى بيمينه لم يجز وكان له أن يستحلفه لأن الشراء عقد تمليك المال بالمال واليمين ليست بمال؛ كذا في النهاية وظاهر ما قرره الشارح أن أخذ المال الفداء والصلح عن اليمين إنما يحل إذا كان المدعي محقاً ليكون المأخوذ في حقه بدلاً كما في الصلح عن الإنكار، فلو كان مبطلاً لم يحل ، والضمير في «منها عائد إلى يمينه أي بدلها. وفي شرح مسكين : ثم الافتداء قد يكون بمال بمثل المدعي وقد يكون بأقل منه، وأما الصلح فإنما يكون منه على مال هو أقل من المدعي غالباً ؛ كذا في النهاية اهـ. قيد بالإسقاط ضمن الافتداء والصلح لأن إسقاطها قصداً غير صحيح لما في دعوى البزازية آخر الرابع عشر : قال المدعي برئت من الحلف أو تركت عليه الحلف أو وهبت لا يصح وله التحليف بخلاف البراءة عن المال لأن التحليف للحاكم اهـ. والله أعلم.
If the defendant ransoms his oath or settles with something in lieu of it, it is valid and he does not take an oath afterwards. He said: (If the defendant ransoms his oath or settles with something in lieu of it, it is valid and he does not take an oath afterwards) As for permissibility, it is based on what was reported from Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, that someone claimed forty dirhams against him, so he gave something and ransomed his oath and did not swear. And from Hudhayfah that he ransomed his oath with wealth. And because if he swore, it would lead to gossip, for people are either believing or disbelieving. So if he ransoms his oath, he has protected his honor, and that is good. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Protect your honor with your wealth.' And al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned that avoiding a truthful oath is obligatory. His intent is established by the permissibility of swearing truthfully. He does not swear afterwards because he has ended the dispute by taking compensation. It is restricted to ransom and settlement because if he bought his oath, it would not be valid, and he could still ask him to swear, because a sale is a contract of exchanging property for property, and an oath is not property. This is in al-Nihayah. It is apparent from what the commentator established that taking money as ransom or settlement for an oath is only permissible if the plaintiff is truthful, so that what is taken is a substitute for his right, as in settlement upon denial. If the plaintiff is false, it is not permissible. The pronoun 'from it' refers to his oath, meaning its substitute. In Sharh Miskin: Then ransom may be with property equal to what is claimed or less. As for settlement, it is usually with less than what is claimed. This is in al-Nihayah. It is restricted to dropping within ransom and settlement because dropping it intentionally is not valid. In Da'wa al-Bazzaziyyah at the end of the fourteenth: If the plaintiff says 'I am free from the oath' or 'I abandon the oath' or 'I gift it,' it is not valid, and he can still ask for the oath, unlike release from property because requesting the oath is for the judge. Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This lesson presents the ruling that a defendant in a legal claim may pay the claimant to avoid having to take an oath. The commentator explains that this is permissible based on reports from Uthman and Hudhayfa (may Allah be pleased with them) and the hadith 'Protect your honor with your wealth.' The ruling is conditional: the plaintiff must be truthful, otherwise taking money to avoid an oath is not permissible. The defendant cannot buy his oath (e.g., paying for it as a sale) because an oath is not property. After ransoming, he does not swear because he has ended the dispute by paying compensation.

Key Terms
المنكر (al-munkir): The defendant who denies the claim.
اليمين (al-yamin): The oath taken by the defendant to deny the claim.
الافتداء (al-iftida'): Ransoming the oath by paying the claimant to avoid swearing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ransoming an oath is permissible only when the claimant is truthful, as it protects the defendant's honor and avoids harmful gossip.

Tahaluf: Mutual Oaths in Disputes
Definition of tahaluf and its ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tahaluf, explain why one oath suffices for both parties, and state the consequence of nukul.

Core Idea
In tahaluf, one oath binds both; refusal to swear leads to judgment.

Tahaluf is a legal procedure in Hanafi fiqh for disputes where both parties lack evidence. The core ruling is that the oath of one party relieves the other of the obligation to swear. This lesson unpacks the definition, the reasoning behind the dropping of the oath, and the consequences of refusing to take the oath (nukul).

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its context
Tahaluf refers to mutual oaths in a dispute where both parties are required to swear. Understand that it arises when there is no evidence.
This is a specific procedure in qada (judiciary).
2
Understand the legal effect
When one party takes the oath, the other is relieved from swearing because the definitive oath (al-yamin al-batt) is stronger.
The text says 'wal-tahāluf suqita 'anhu al-halaf'.
3
Analyze the ruling on refusal (nukul)
If the party who is supposed to take the oath refuses, judgment is given against them. The passage resolves a potential confusion: refusal is considered a dropping of the required oath, so judgment follows.
Contrast with the opposite scenario where refusal does not lead to judgment because the oath was not binding.
4
Apply the distinction
Recognize that in the primary case (tahaluf proper), nukul leads to judgment; in the reverse case (where the oath is not binding), nukul does not.
This is an important usul distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب التحالف لما ذكر حكم يمين الواحد ذكر حكم يمين الاثنين إذا الاثنان عبد الواحد. والتحالف سقط عنه الحلف إذ البت أقوى ولو نكل يقضى عليه، وقيل هذا الفرع مشكل اهـ. أقول: وجه الإشكال أنه كيف يقضى عليه أنه لعدم لزومه له فلا يكون بذلاً مكلف إلى البت فنكوله عنه مع غير ولا إقراراً. ويزول الإشكال بأنه مسقط لليمين الواجبة عليه فاعتبر فيكون قضاء بعد نكول عن يمين مسقط للحلف عنه بخلاف عكسه، ولهذا يحلف ثانياً في صورة العكس لعدم سقوط الحلف عنه بها فنكوله عنه لعدم اعتباره والاجتزاء به فلا يقضى عليه بسببه تأمل .
The chapter of mutual oaths. When the ruling for the oath of one person was mentioned, the ruling for the oath of two persons is mentioned when both are servants of one? The mutual oath drops the oath from him because the definitive oath is stronger. If he refuses (nukul), judgment is given against him. It is said this branch is problematic. I say: The problem is how judgment is given against him because it is not binding upon him, so it is not a constrained offer? His refusal with neither [evidence] nor admission? The problem is resolved by [the fact that] it is a dropping of the oath that was obligatory upon him, so it is considered, and thus judgment is given after refusal from an oath that drops the oath from him, unlike its opposite. And for this reason, he swears a second time in the opposite scenario because the oath has not dropped from him due to it, so his refusal due to its non-consideration and not being sufficient does not result in judgment against him. Ponder.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage defines 'tahaluf' (mutual oaths) in the legal context. It explains that when both parties in a dispute are subject to oaths, the one who takes the oath relieves the other of the obligation to swear, because an oath already given is decisive. If the person who is supposed to take the oath refuses (nukul), judgment is rendered against him. There is a scholarly discussion about the logic: if the oath was not binding, how can refusal lead to judgment? The resolution is that the refusal is considered a dropping of the required oath, so judgment follows. In the opposite case, if the oath was not binding, refusal does not result in judgment.

Key Terms
التحالف (al-tahāluf): Mutual oaths taken by two parties in a dispute when they have no evidence.
النكول (al-nukūl): Refusal to take a required oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The other party must also take an oath.
B) The other party is relieved from taking an oath.
C) The case is dismissed.
D) The one who took the oath loses the case.

Key Takeaway
In tahaluf, one definitive oath relieves the other party, and refusal to swear leads to judgment against the refuser.

Mutual Oath After Commodity Perishes: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on mutual oath when the commodity has perished after delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad regarding mutual oath after the commodity perishes, and evaluate the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
After the commodity perishes, there is no mutual oath between buyer and seller, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

When a buyer and seller disagree about the price after the sold item is destroyed, the normal procedure of mutual oath (tahāluf) does not apply, according to the majority of the Hanafi school. This lesson examines the dispute between the three primary Hanafi imams and the reasoning behind each view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context of the dispute
The dispute occurs after the sold commodity has perished (halak) following delivery. The parties disagree on the price and lack evidence. Determine whether the usual mutual oath procedure applies.
Halak here means destruction after the buyer has taken possession.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
According to them, there is no mutual oath. The statement of the buyer is taken (with his oath) unless a third party consumed the item in the seller's hand.
3
State the position of Muhammad al-Shaybani
Muhammad holds that they must take mutual oaths and the sale is rescinded at the value of the destroyed item.
4
Analyse the dalīl of each side
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that mutual oath after delivery is contrary to analogy; it was permitted only when the item is extant, leading to rescission. After destruction, rescission is impossible. Muhammad analogizes to the extant case because the dispute remains about a contractual term.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Muhammad's view, the contract is rescinded at the value of the destroyed item, which may result in a different financial outcome. Under the majority view, the buyer's statement is accepted, simplifying resolution.
The mufta bihi is the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا اختلفا بعد هلاك المبيع فلا تحالف عندهما والقول قول المشتري إلا إذا استهلكه في يد البائع غير المشتري كما سنذكره عند قوله وإن اختلفا في مقدار الثمن بعد الإقالة». وقال محمد يتحالفان ويفسخ البيع على قيمة الهالك، وعلى هذا إذا خرج المبيع عن ملكه أو صار بحال لا يقدر على رده بالعيب له أن كل واحد منهما يدعي غير العقد الذي يدعيه صاحبه والآخر ينكره، وأنه يفيد دفع زيادة

قوله : (وأما إذا اختلفا بعد هلاك المبيع) قال في معراج الدراية : قوله «فإن هلك المبيع» أي بعد قبض الثمن إذ قبل قبضه ينفسخ العقد بهلاكه ثم اختلفا أي في مقدار الثمن ؛ هكذا ذكر في المبسوط اهـ
And as for if they differ after the destruction of the sold item, there is no mutual oath according to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), and the statement is that of the buyer unless he consumed it in the hand of a seller other than the buyer, as we will mention at his statement: 'And if they differ about the amount of the price after revocation.' And Muhammad said: They take mutual oaths and the sale is rescinded at the value of the destroyed item. And upon this, if the sold item leaves his ownership or becomes in a state where he cannot return it due to a defect, each one of them claims a contract other than what his companion claims and the other denies it, and it serves to repel an increase.

His statement: (And as for if they differ after the destruction of the sold item) He said in Mi'raj al-Dirayah: His statement 'So if the sold item perishes' means after receipt of the price, because before its receipt the contract is dissolved by its destruction, then they differed meaning about the amount of the price; thus it is mentioned in al-Mabsut. End.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on one side, and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the other) regarding mutual oath (tahaluf) when the buyer and seller disagree after the sold commodity has perished after delivery. The base matn states that according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), there is no mutual oath; the statement is that of the buyer, unless a third party consumed the item in the seller's hand. Muhammad holds that mutual oath is required, and the sale is rescinded at the value of the destroyed item. The commentator explains that the disagreement pertains to the amount of the price after the item has perished after receipt of the price. The passage also notes an extension: if the sold item leaves the buyer's ownership or becomes unreturnable due to a change (increase/defect), a similar principle applies. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is based on the idea that mutual oath after delivery is contrary to analogy because the buyer already has possession; the Shariah permitted it only when the item is still extant, leading to rescission. After destruction, the contract is already ended, so no need for oath.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahāluf): Mutual oath-taking between claimant and defendant when both have no evidence and the court imposes an oath on both, resulting in rescission of the contract.
هلاك (halāk): Perishing or destruction of the sold commodity, after which the contract may be affected.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), what is the ruling when buyer and seller differ about the price after the sold commodity perishes after delivery?
A) They take mutual oaths and the sale is rescinded at the value of the destroyed item.
B) No mutual oath; the statement of the buyer is taken (with his oath).
C) The contract is automatically void and no action is needed.
D) The price is set by a third-party appraiser.

Key Takeaway
After the commodity perishes, the mufta bihi Hanafi position is that no mutual oath is required; the buyer's statement prevails.

Disagreement When the Commodity Has Changed: The Hanafi Intra-School Ikhtilaf on Mutual Oath
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mutual oath after increase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on mutual oath after a connected increase in the sold commodity, and apply the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A connected increase that prevents return eliminates mutual oath in Hanafi fiqh, according to the two imams.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq details an intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether mutual oath (tahaluf) applies when the sold commodity has undergone a change or increase after delivery. The core issue is whether the increase is such that the item cannot be returned in its original state. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that no mutual oath is required; the buyer's statement is taken. Muhammad al-Shaybani insists on mutual oath with rescission at value. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for judges and muftis handling disputes over changed goods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine that the buyer and seller differ about the price after the sold item has been changed (e.g., wheat ground into flour, land built upon).
The change must be a connected increase that physically alters the item.
2
Classify the increase
Is the increase connected and originating from the essence (ziyada muttaṣila mansha'uha al-dhat) or is it external (e.g., price fluctuation)? Only the former triggers the ikhtilaf.
Examples of essence increases: child from slave-girl, compensation for defect. Examples of external: rise in market price.
3
Apply the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
If the increase is connected and prevents return, then no mutual oath. The buyer's statement about the price is accepted.
This is the mufta bihi position.
4
Apply the ruling of Muhammad
According to Muhammad, mutual oath is still required, and the sale is rescinded at the current value unless the buyer chooses to return the item with the increase.
Note the practical difference: under Muhammad's view, the seller can recover more if the value increased.
5
Analyze the dalil
The purpose of mutual oath is rescission. If the item cannot be returned due to the increase, rescission is impossible, so the oath is not applicable. Muhammad counters that rescission at value is still possible.
This is a classical talil (reasoning) based on the purpose of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أو صار بحال لا يقدر على رده بالعيب قال في الكفاية بأن زاد زيادة متصلة أو منفصلة. وفي شرح درر البحار أو تغير إلى زيادة منشؤها الذات بعد القبض متصلة كانت أو منفصلة كولد وأرش وعقر، وإذا تحالفا عند محمد يفسخ على القيمة إلا إذا اختار المشتري رد العين مع الزيادة. ولو لم تنشأ من الذات سواء كانت من حيث السعر أو غيره كانت قبل القبض أو بعده يتحالفان اتفاقاً ويكون الكسب للمشتري اتفاقاً .اهـ. قال الرملي : وقد صرحوا بأن الزيادة المتصلة بالمبيع التي لم تتولد من الأصل مانعة من الرد كالغرس والبناء وطحن الحنطة وشي اللحم وخبز الدقيق، فإذا وجد شيء ذلك لا تحالف عندهما خلافاً لمحمد والله تعالى أعلم. ولم يذكر هذا الشارح ولا غالب الشروح من والفتاوى اختلافهما بعد الزيادة ولا بعد موت المتعاقدين أو أحدهما مع شدة الحاجة إلى ذلك، وقد ذكر ذلك مفصلاً في التتارخانية فارجع إليه إن شئت. ثم بحثت في الكتب فرأيت ابن ملك قال في شرح المجمع ؛ اعلم أن مسألة التغير مذكورة في المنظومة وقد أهملها المصنف ثم تغيره إلى زيادة إن كان من حيث الذات بعد القبض متصلة كانت أو منفصلة متولدة من عينها كالولد أو بدل العين كالأرش والعقر يتحالفان عند محمد خلافاً لهما، وإذا تحالفا يترادان القيمة عنده إلا إن شاء المشتري أن يرد العين مع الزيادة. وقيل: يترادان رضي المشتري أو لا قيدنا الزيادة بقولنا من حيث الذات كانت من حيث السعر يتحالفان سواء كان قبل القبض أو بعده وقيدنا بقولنا متولدة من عينها لأنها لو لم تكن كذلك يتحالفان اتفاقاً ويكون الكسب للمشتري عندهم جميعاً. وفي التتارخانية وفي التجريد؛ وإن وقع الاختلاف بين ورثتهما أو بين ورثة أحدهما وبين الحي فإن كان قبل قبض السلعة يتحالفان بالإجماع وفي شرح الطحاوي إلا أن اليمين على الورثة على العلم وإن كان بعد القبض فكذلك عند محمد وعلى قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف لا يتحالفان. وفي شرح الطحاوي : والقول قول المشتري أو قول ورثته بعد وفاته. وفيها وفي الخلاصة رجل اشترى شيئاً فمات البائع أو المشتري ووقع الاختلاف في الثمن بين الحي وورثة الميت إن مات البائع ؛ فإن كانت السلعة في يد الورثة يتحالفان وإن كانت السلعة في يد الحي لا يتحالفان عندهما . وقال محمد: يتحالفان. هذا إذا يوجب الثمن فيتحالفان كما إذا اختلفا في جنس الثمن بعد هلاك السلعة ولهما أن التحالف بعد القبض على خلاف القياس لما أنه سلم للمشتري ما يدعيه وقد ورد الشرع به في حال قيام السلعة والتحالف فيه يفضي إلى الفسخ ولا كذلك بعد هلاكها لارتفاع العقد فلم يكن في معناه، ولأنه لا يبالي بالاختلاف في السبب بعد حصول المقصود، وإنما يراعى من الفائدة ما العقد، وفائدة دفع زيادة الثمن ليست من موجباته. وهذا إذا كان الثمن ديناً، فإن كان عيناً يتحالفان لأن المبيع كل منهما فكان قائماً ببقاء المعقود عليه فيرده ويرد الآخر مثل الهالك إذا كان مثلياً وقيمته إن كان قيمياً بخلاف ما إذا اختلفا في جنس الثمن بأن ادعى أحدهما أنه دراهم والآخر أنه دنانير لأنهما لم يتفقا على ثمن فلا بد من التحالف للفسخ، وهنا اتفقا عليه وهو كاف للصحة. وبهذا علم أن الاختلاف في جنس الثمن كالاختلاف في قدره إلا في مسألة هي ما إذا كان المبيع هالكاً. وفي الظهيرية إبراهيم عن محمد في رجل اشترى تبناً في موضعين بكذا درهماً وقبض تبن أحد الموضعين وذهب الريح بتبن الموضع الآخر واختلفا في مقدار ما قبض وما ذهب، فإن كان ما قبض قائماً تحالفا أو ترادا وإن كان مستهلكاً فالقول قول المشتري في قياس قول أبي حنيفة . وقال محمد يتحالفان ويرد المشتري مثل ما أخذ من التبن والقول فيه قوله اهـ. وفي إيضاح الكرماني : لو اختلفا بعد هلاك الجارية في يد المشتري فادعى البائع أن الثمن عين هذا العبد أو ادعى المشتري أن الثمن عين وادعى البائع أن الثمن دين لم ينظر إلى دعوى البائع وإنما ينظر إلى دعوى المشتري لأن المبيع في جانب البائع هالك فكان القول في الثمن قول المشتري، فإن أقر بالدين فالقول قوله، وإن أقر بالعين يتحالفا لأن المبيع في جانبه قائم . ولو تحالفا وقد هلك أحد العوضين من يد الآخر رد مثله إن كان مثلياً، وقيمته إن لم يكن له مثل لأن العقد قد انفسخ فبقي مقبوضاً من غير عقد فصار كالغاصب اهـ. وفي كافي المصنف : ادعى شراء أمة قبضها وماتت بألف وبهذا الوصيف وقيمته خمسمائة وقال البائع وهو مات البائع، فإن مات المشتري والسلعة في يد البائع يتحالفان عند الكل وإن كانت السلعة في يد ورثة المشتري عندهما لا يتحالفان وعلى قول محمد يتحالفان. وهلاك العاقد بمنزلة المعقود عليه. وممن ذكر مسألة التغير بالزيادة والنقص الاختيار والمنهاج والتغير بالعيب الدرر والغرر والله أعلم. واقعة حال اختلف المشتري مع الوكيل بقبض الثمن هل يجري التحالف بينهما؟ وقد كتبت الجواب : لا يجري إذ الوكيل بالقبض لا يحلف وإن ملك الخصومة عند الإمام فيدفع الثمن الذي أقر به له، وإذا حضر الموكل المباشر للعقد وطلبه بالزيادة يتحالفان حينئذ اهـ . قوله : (بخلاف ما إذا اختلفا جنس الثمن) أي بعد هلاك المبيع وهذا مقابل لقوله وأما إذا اختلفا بعد هلاك المبيع الخ» فإن هناك في الاختلاف في مقدار الثمن كما قدمناه عن المعراج فصحت المقابلة قوله : (وبهذا علم) أي بقوله بخلاف ما إذا اختلفا جنس الثمن .
His statement: (Or it becomes in a state where he cannot return it due to a defect) He said in al-Kifayah: that it increased by a connected or disconnected increase. And in Sharh Durar al-Bihar: or it changed to an increase originating from the essence after delivery, whether connected or disconnected, like a child, compensation for defect, or dower. And when they take mutual oaths according to Muhammad, it is rescinded at the value unless the buyer chooses to return the item with the increase. And if it did not originate from the essence, whether from price or otherwise, before or after delivery, they take mutual oaths by agreement, and the gain is for the buyer by agreement. End. Al-Ramli said: They have explicitly stated that the increase connected to the sold item that did not generate from the original prevents return, such as planting, building, grinding wheat, roasting meat, and baking flour. So if such exists, there is no mutual oath according to them (the two), contrary to Muhammad. And Allah knows best. This commentator and most commentaries and fatwas did not mention their difference after the increase nor after the death of the two contractors or one of them, despite the great need for that. It has been mentioned in detail in al-Tatarkhaniyya, so refer to it if you wish. Then I researched the books and saw that Ibn Malik said in Sharh al-Majma': Know that the issue of change is mentioned in al-Manẓūma, but the author (of Kanz) neglected it. Then its change to an increase if it is from the essence after delivery, connected or disconnected, generated from the item itself like a child or a substitute for the item like compensation for defect and dower, they take mutual oaths according to Muhammad contrary to the two. And when they take mutual oaths, they return the value according to him unless the buyer wishes to return the item with the increase. And it is said: they return whether the buyer is pleased or not. We restricted the increase by our saying 'from the essence' because if it was from price, they take mutual oaths whether before or after delivery, and we restricted by our saying 'generated from the item itself' because if it were not so, they take mutual oaths by agreement and the gain is for the buyer according to all of them. And in al-Tatarkhaniyya and al-Tajrid: If the difference occurs between their heirs or between the heirs of one of them and the living person, then if it is before receipt of the goods, they take mutual oaths by consensus; and in Sharh al-Tahawi, except that the oath is upon the heirs regarding knowledge. And if it is after receipt, then similarly according to Muhammad, but according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf they do not take mutual oaths. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: The statement is that of the buyer or his heirs after his death. And in it and in al-Khulasa: A man bought something, and the seller or buyer died, and a difference occurred about the price between the living person and the heirs of the deceased. If the seller died and the goods are in the hands of the heirs, they take mutual oaths; if the goods are in the hands of the living, they do not take mutual oaths according to the two. And Muhammad said: They take mutual oaths. This is when the price is due, so they take mutual oaths just as if they differed about the type of the price after the destruction of the goods. And for them (the two), mutual oath after receipt is contrary to analogy because he has delivered to the buyer what he claims, and the Shariah allowed it only when the goods are extant, and mutual oath leads to rescission; not so after destruction because the contract is no longer effective, so it is not in the same meaning. And because he does not care about the difference in the cause after the purpose is achieved; only the benefit of the contract is considered, and the benefit of repelling an increase in the price is not from its requirements. This is if the price is a debt. If it is a specific item (ayn), they take mutual oaths because each of them claims the sold item, so it remains as if the subject matter is extant; so he returns it and the other returns the like of the destroyed item if it is fungible, or its value if it is non-fungible. Unlike if they differed about the type of the price, e.g., one claimed it was dirhams and the other dinars, because they did not agree on a price, so mutual oath is necessary for rescission. Here they agreed on it, which is sufficient for validity. By this it is known that the difference about the type of the price is like the difference about its amount except in one issue: when the sold item is destroyed. And in al-Zahiriya: Ibrahim from Muhammad: A man bought straw in two places for such-and-such dirhams, and took receipt of the straw from one place, and the wind carried away the straw from the other place, and they differed about the amount of what he received and what was lost. If what he received is extant, they take mutual oaths and return; if it is consumed, the statement is that of the buyer by the analogy of Abu Hanifa's view. And Muhammad said: They take mutual oaths, and the buyer returns the like of what he took of straw, and the statement is his. End. And in Idah al-Kirmani: If they differ after the destruction of the slave-girl in the buyer's hand, and the seller claims the price is this specific slave, or the buyer claims the price is a specific item and the seller claims it is a debt, then the seller's claim is not considered; only the buyer's claim is considered because the sold item is destroyed on the seller's side, so the statement about the price is the buyer's. If he admits it is a debt, his statement stands; if he admits it is a specific item, they take mutual oaths because the sold item on his side is extant. And if they take mutual oaths and one of the two considerations has perished in the hand of the other, he returns its like if fungible, or its value if not fungible, because the contract is rescinded so what was received is held without contract, thus becomes like a usurper. End. And in Kafi al-Musannaf: He claimed he bought a slave-girl, took possession, and she died for a thousand, and he had this child whose value was five hundred, and the seller said... And the seller died... If the buyer died and the goods are in the seller's hand, they take mutual oaths according to all; if the goods are in the hands of the buyer's heirs, according to the two they do not take mutual oaths, and according to Muhammad they do. The death of the contractor is like the destruction of the subject matter. Among those who mentioned the issue of change by increase and decrease are al-Ikhtiyar and al-Minhaj, and change by defect: al-Durar wa al-Ghurar. And Allah knows best. An actual case: the buyer differed with the agent for receiving the price; does mutual oath occur between them? I have written the answer: It does not occur because the agent for receipt does not take an oath even if he has the right of litigation according to the Imam; he pays the price he admitted. And when the principal (the actual contractor) appears and demands an increase, then they take mutual oaths. End. His statement: (Unlike if they differed about the type of the price) meaning after the destruction of the sold item, and this is contrasted with his statement: 'And as for if they differ after the destruction of the sold item...' because there the difference is about the amount of the price as we presented from al-Mi'raj, so the contrast is valid. His statement: (And by this it is known) meaning by his statement 'unlike if they differed about the type of the price'.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This lengthy passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding mutual oath (tahaluf) when the sold commodity has undergone change or increase after delivery. The commentator expands on the matn phrase 'أو صار بحال لا يقدر على رده بالعيب' (or it becomes in a state where he cannot return it due to a defect). The key distinction is whether the increase originates from the essence (dhāt) of the item (like a child, compensation for defect) or from price fluctuations or other external factors. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if there is a connected increase that prevents return (like planting, building, grinding), then no mutual oath is required; the statement of the buyer is taken. Muhammad holds that mutual oath is still required, and the sale is rescinded at the value unless the buyer chooses to return the item with the increase. The passage also discusses scenarios involving heirs (when one party dies) and when the price is a specific item (ayn) vs a debt. It explains that if the price is a specific item, mutual oath is required because the subject matter is considered extant by analogy. Numerous subcases are given, such as the case of straw (tibn) destroyed by wind, cases involving a slave-girl and a child, and cases with agents. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: no mutual oath after the commodity changes or perishes, except in specific exceptions mentioned.

Key Terms
زيادة متصلة (ziyādah muttaṣilah): A connected increase to the sold item that becomes inseparable, such as grinding wheat into flour, planting trees, or building a wall.
زيادة منشؤها الذات (ziyādah mansha'uhā al-dhāt): An increase that originates from the essence of the item itself, like a child from a slave-girl or compensation for a defect (arsh).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if a buyer turns wheat into flour after purchase and then differs with the seller about the price, what is the ruling?
A) They take mutual oaths and the sale is rescinded at the value of flour.
B) No mutual oath; the statement of the buyer is taken.
C) The sale is void and the flour must be returned.
D) The price is set by an expert appraiser.

Key Takeaway
When a connected increase prevents return of the sold item, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule no mutual oath; the buyer's statement is taken.

Wife's Admission That Husband Bought Household Items
Ruling on wife's claim of ownership after admitting husband's purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a wife admits her husband purchased household items and explain the evidentiary requirement for her to claim ownership.

Core Idea
A wife's admission of the husband's purchase bars her later claim of ownership without independent proof of transfer.

In marital property disputes, a wife's own words can determine ownership. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on a wife's admission that her husband bought household items, and the evidentiary burden she faces to claim them. Understanding this rule is critical for judges and parties in family law matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key admission
Recognize that the wife's admission that her husband purchased the item is a confession (iqrar) binding against her interest. In Hanafi law, a competent person's iqrar is conclusive against themselves.
Iqrar is a legal acknowledgment that creates a presumption against the declarant.
2
Understand the consequence of admission
Once she admits the husband's purchase, she cannot later claim that she owns the item unless she brings legal evidence (bayyinah) of a transfer (e.g., gift, sale) from the husband.
The admission establishes the husband's ownership; a subsequent inconsistent claim is disregarded without proof.
3
Recognize insufficient evidence
Her mere use of the item or the husband's consent to her use does not prove ownership transfer. The author emphasizes this is a common error among laypeople.
Practical application: judges should not infer a gift from prolonged use alone.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
If the wife claims she bought the item from the husband, the same rule applies: she must prove that transaction. Similarly, if the husband himself proves purchase, the wife's burden remains.
The ruling is symmetrical: the wife's admission or the husband's proof of purchase both trigger the need for evidence of transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا كله إذا لم تقر المرأة أن هذا المتاع اشتراه فإن أقرت بذلك سقط قولها لأنها أقرت بالملك لزوجها ثم ادعت الانتقال إليها فلا يثبت الانتقال إلا بالبينة .اهـ وكذا إذا ادعت أنها اشترته منه كما في الخانية، ولا يخفى أنه لو برهن على شرائه كان كإقرارها بشرائه منه فلا بد من بينة على الانتقال إليها منه بهبة أو نحو ذلك، ولا يكون استمتاعها بمشريه ورضاه بذلك دليلاً على أنه ملكها ذلك كما تفهمه النساء والعوام، وقد أفتيت بذلك مراراً.
All this is when the woman does not admit that this item was purchased by him. If she admits that, her statement falls because she admitted ownership for her husband then claimed transfer to her; transfer is not established except by proof. Similarly, if she claims she bought it from him, as in al-Khaniyah. And it is not hidden that if he provides proof of his purchase, it is like her admission of his purchase, so there must be proof of transfer to her from him by gift or similar. And her enjoyment of what he buys and his pleasure with that is not evidence that he gave it to her as ownership, as women and commoners understand. And I have given fatwa to that effect repeatedly.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on marital property disputes. The author explains that if a wife admits that her husband bought a particular household item, she cannot later claim that she owns it unless she brings evidence (bayyinah) that he transferred it to her (e.g., by gift). Even if she enjoys the item and he is pleased, that does not prove ownership transfer. This follows the Hanafi principle that admission against one's interest is binding, and ownership is presumed to remain with the original buyer unless proven otherwise. The author notes he has repeatedly given fatwa on this point, indicating its practical importance.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Admission or confession against one's own interest
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a woman admits her husband bought a household item, what must she provide to prove she now owns it?
A) Her mere claim
B) Evidence of transfer (e.g., gift) from the husband
C) Her husband's silence
D) An oath

Key Takeaway
A wife's admission that her husband purchased household items shifts the burden of proof to her to show transfer; her use alone is insufficient to establish ownership.

Exceptions to the General Rule on Household Property Disputes
Rulings on special cases of property disputes beyond the husband-wife norm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six exceptions to the general rule that household goods belong to the husband, applying the correct ruling for each scenario involving multiple wives, father-daughter dowry, father-son, artisans, landlord-tenant, and non-household goods.

Core Idea
The rule for household goods shifts based on relationships, possession, and custom — six distinct exceptions.

The general rule in Hanafi fiqh for household property disputes between spouses is that suitable items belong to the husband. However, this ruling is not absolute. The Hanafi jurists carved out several exceptions based on the specific relationship, possession, and prevailing custom. In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author lists these exceptions to provide a complete picture of how a judge should rule in each unique scenario.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Recall the default ruling: in a dispute between spouses over household goods, the husband's statement is preferred regarding items suitable for both.
This rule is the backdrop against which the exceptions are defined.
2
Analyze the Exception for Multiple Wives
If a man has multiple wives living in one house, their goods are shared equally; if each wife lives separately, her goods are only between her and her husband.
Co-wives share equally only if they share a residence.
3
Analyze the Father-Daughter Dowry Exception
The ruling depends on custom (urf): if the father customarily gives the dowry as outright ownership, the daughter owns it; if custom is mixed, the father gets it.
This is a detailed example of urf as a source of law.
4
Analyze the Father-Son Exception
Abu Yusuf's view: if one party is dependent on the other in the same house, the household goods belong to the owner of the house (the one not dependent).
Al-Khaniyah adopts Abu Yusuf's view.
5
Analyze the Artisan Tools Exception
When a shoemaker and a perfumer dispute over tools that could be used by either, the tools are divided equally regardless of which profession they suit.
Possibility of sale or personal use eliminates preference.
6
Analyze the Landlord-Tenant Exception
If the lessor and lessee dispute over goods in the rented house, the lessee's statement is preferred because he is in possession.
Possession by tenancy gives the tenant the benefit of the doubt.
7
Analyze the Non-Household Goods Exception
If spouses dispute over items that are not household goods and both have possession, they are treated as strangers and the items are divided equally.
This falls back to general principles of evidence.
8
Synthesize the Common Theme
Each exception relies on a specific legal logic: co-residence, urf, dependency, potential alternative use, possession, or nature of the item.
The exceptions flesh out the general rule with nuance.

وقيد باختلاف الزوجين للاحتراز عن اختلاف نساء الزوج دونه فإن متاع النساء بينهن على السواء إن كن في بيت واحد، وإن كانت كل واحدة منهن في بيت على حدة فما في بيت كل امرأة بينها وبين زوجها على ما وصفنا ولا يشترك بعضهن مع بعض؛ كذا في خزانة الأكمل والخانية، وللاحتراز عن اختلاف الأب مع بنته في جهازها وقد بيناه في النكاح. وحاصله أن المفتى به أن العرف إن كان مستمراً أن الأب يجهزها ملكاً لا عارية فالقول لها ولورثتها من بعدها، وإن كان العرف مشتركاً كعرف مصر فالقول للأب ولورثته من بعده، وللاحتراز عن اختلاف الأب وابنه فيما في البيت . قال في الخزانة قال أبو يوسف : إذا كان الأب في عيال الابن في بيته فالمتاع كله للابن كما لو كان الابن في بيت الأب وعياله فمتاع البيت للأب اهـ. ثم قال قال محمد: رجل زوج بنته وهي وختنه في داره وعياله ثم اختلفوا في متاع البيت فهو للأب لأنه في بيته وفي يده ولهم ما عليهم من الثياب اهـ. وجزم في الخانية بما قاله أبو يوسف، وللاحتراز أيضاً عن إسكافي وعطار اختلفا في آلة الأساكفة أو آلة العطارين وهي في أيديهما فإنه يقضى به بينهما ولا ينظر إلى ما يصلح لأحدهما لأنه قد يتخذه لنفسه أو للبيع فلا يصلح مرجحاً، وللاحتراز عما إذا اختلف المؤجر والمستأجر في متاع البيت فإن القول فيه للمستأجر لكون البيت مضافاً إليه بالسكنى وهما في شرح الزيلعي، وللاحتراز عن الزوجين في غير متاع البيت وكان في أيديهما فإنهما كالأجنبيين يقسم بينهما.
And it is restricted to the dispute of the spouses to exclude the dispute of the husband's wives without him: the goods of the wives are equally among them if they are in one house; if each is in a separate house, what is in each wife's house is between her and her husband as described, and they do not share with each other. And to exclude the dispute of the father with his daughter over her dowry, which we explained in marriage. Its summary: the mufta bihi is that if the custom is continuous that the father gives it as ownership not loan, then the statement is hers and her heirs after her; if the custom is mixed like the custom of Egypt, then the statement is for the father and his heirs after him. And to exclude the dispute of the father and son over what is in the house. He said in al-Khizanah: Abu Yusuf said: If the father is dependent on the son in his house, all goods are for the son, just as if the son is in the father's house and dependent, then the house's goods are for the father. Then he said: Muhammad said: A man married his daughter and she and her husband are in his house and dependent, then they dispute over the house goods, it is for the father because he is in his house and in his possession; they have what they are wearing. In al-Khaniyah he affirmed what Abu Yusuf said. And to exclude also the case of a shoemaker and a perfumer disputing over tools of shoemaking or perfumery while they are in their possession: judgment is between them equally, and no consideration is given to what suits one of them because he may have taken it for himself or for sale, so it does not serve as a preponderance. And to exclude the case of the lessor and lessee disputing over goods in the house: the statement is for the lessee because the house is attached to him by tenancy, as in Sharh al-Zayla'i. And to exclude the spouses over non-household goods while in their possession: they are like two strangers and it is divided between them.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage lists exceptions to the general rule that the husband gets household items suitable for both. First, if a man has multiple wives living together, their goods are shared equally; if separate, each wife's goods are only between her and her husband. Second, for a father and daughter disputing over her dowry, the ruling depends on custom: if the father usually gives it outright, she owns it; if custom is mixed (like Egypt), the father gets it. Third, for a father and son living together, Abu Yusuf's view (adopted in al-Khaniyah) is that the dependent party's household belongs to the house owner. Muhammad's view differs slightly. Fourth, for artisans like shoemakers and perfumers disputing over tools, the tools are divided equally regardless of which profession they suit, because they might be for sale. Fifth, for landlord-tenant disputes over house goods, the tenant gets them. Sixth, for spouses disputing over non-household items in their joint possession, they are treated as strangers and the items are divided. Each exception has its own reasoning based on custom, possession, or general principles of evidence.

Key Terms
عرف (urf): Customary practice, used as a basis for legal judgment in Hanafi fiqh
جهاز (jahaz): Dowry or trousseau given by a father to his daughter

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if two co-wives live in the same house and dispute over household goods, what is the ruling?
A) The goods belong to the husband.
B) The goods are divided equally between the two wives.
C) Each wife gets the goods that are in her own room.
D) The judge decides based on custom.

Key Takeaway
The general rule on household goods is subject to multiple exceptions based on co-residence, custom, dependency, and nature of the items.
