إليه حتى
قوله : (وهي عجيبة أقول : تقدمت المسألة متناً في آخر كتاب الوكالة قبل باب عزل الوكيل ووجهها أنه إقرار على الغير وهو رب الوديعة فلا يسلمها إلى مدعي الوكالة بالقبض أو الشراء بخلاف ما لو كان مديون الغائب وادعى عليه شخص الوكالة بالقبض وصدقه فإنه يدفع إليه لأن الديون
اشترى هو منه لإنكار ذي اليد ولا من جهة وكيله لإنكار المدعي وكذا لو ثبت بالبينة أنه دفعها إلى الوكيل ولم يشهدوا أن الموكل دفعها إلى ذي اليد؛ ذكره الشارح. وظاهر قوله سقطت السقوط يلا بينة ويمين. وفي البناية : ولو طلب المدعي يمينه على الإيداع يحلف على البنات اهـ. وتقيد المؤلف بدعوى الشراء من الغائب اتفاقي ففي البزازية معزياً إلى الذخيرة : ادعى أنه له غصبه منه فلان الغائب وبرهن عليه وزعم ذو اليد أن هذا الغائب أودعه عنده تندفع لاتفاقهما على وصول العين من غيره وأن صاحب اليد ذلك الرجل بخلاف ما لو كان مكان دعوى الغصب دعوى السرقة فإنه لا يندفع بزعم ذي اليد إيداع ذلك الغائب في الاستحسان ا.هـ. وقد سئلت بعد تأليف هذا المحل بيوم عن رجل أخذ متاع أخته من بيتها ورهنه وغاب فادعت الأخت به على ذي اليد فأجابه ،بالرهن فأجبت إن ادعت المرأة غصب أخيها وبرهن ذو اليد على الرهن اندفعت وإن ادعت السرقة لا . وفي البزازية قبله معزياً إلى الذخيرة أيضاً : برهن على أنه وديعة عنده من جهة الميت الذي يدعي الوصية منه أو من غصبه منه فلا خصومة بينهما لأنهما تصادقا على وصول المال من جهة الميت إما غصب وإما أمانة فلا تكون يده يد الخصومة في حق من يدعي تلقي الملك منه، وفرق بين الوصية والوراثة فلو برهن في دعوى الوراثة أنه وديعة عنده من قبل المورث الذي يدعي منه الوارثة لا يندفع، في دعوى الوصية كما ذكرنا يندفع حتى يحضر الوارث أو الوصي ا هـ . باتحاد الغائب لأنه لو ادعى الشراء من فلان الغائب المالك وبرهن ذو اليد على إيداع غائب منه لا تندفع كما لو ادعى الإيداع من غير الوصي أو الغصب منه فإنه خصم إلا أن يبرهن على مقاله وقال البلخي : لا تندفع وإن برهن كمسألة الشراء؛ كذا في البزازية والله أعلم بالصواب .
آخر
هو
وقيدنا
تقضى بأمثالها فكان إقراراً على نفسه لا على الغائب فانظر ما وجه العجب قوله : (فإنه لا يندفع بزعم ذي اليد إيداع ذلك الغائب في الاستحسان قال في نور العين يقول الحقير : لعل وجه الاستحسان أن الغصب إزالة اليد المحقة بإثبات اليد المبطلة كما ذكر في كتب الفقه فاليد للغاصب في مسألة الغصب بخلاف مسألة السرقة إذ اليد فيها لذي اليد إذ لا يد للسارق شرعاً، ثم إن عبارة لا يد. للسارق نكتة لا يخفى حسنها على ذوي النهي اهـ قوله : ( وإن ادعت السرقة لا) أي لا تندفع وظاهره أنها ادعت سرقة أخيها وقد مر قريباً أنه لو ادعى الفعل على غير ذي اليد فدفعه ذو اليد بواحد مما ذكرناه وبرهن فإنها تندفع كدعوى الملك المطلق فيحمل كلامه هنا على أنها ادعت أنه سرق منها مبنياً للمجهول ليكون دعوى الفعل على ذي اليد وإن أبقى على ظاهره يكون جرياً على مقابل الاستحسان
المذكور آنفاً .
Book of Claims, Chapter of Mutual Oath-taking. The plaintiff says: 'He stole from me,' and the possessor says: 'So-and-so deposited it with me,' and he provides proof, [the case] is not dismissed. But if the plaintiff says: 'I bought it from So-and-so,' and the possessor says: 'So-and-so deposited it with me,' then the lawsuit falls. [The defendant] becomes a party to the claim of an act against him. If he proves the plaintiff's admission that the absentee deposited it with him, it is dismissed; if it is not dismissed by producing evidence of the deposit, because the plaintiff's admission that his hand is not a hand of contention is established. The mention of usurpation and theft is an example; the intent is a claim of an act against him. So if the plaintiff says: 'I deposited it with you' or 'I bought it from you' in a manner that does not grant ownership of the corpus, it is not dismissed; as in al-Bazzaziyya. If the plaintiff says: 'It is my property and in his hand wrongfully,' it is not a claim of usurpation, so it is dismissed if he proves the deposit in the mentioned manner; as in al-Bazzaziyya also. The restriction to a claim of an act against the possessor is to exclude his claim against another. So if the possessor rebuts with one of the things we mentioned and provides proof, it is dismissed, like an absolute claim of ownership, as in al-Bazzaziyya. The commentator mentioned: If he claimed that he bought it from the possessor and took delivery and paid the price, and the possessor produces evidence that So-and-so deposited it with him, the lawsuit is dismissed, because the plaintiff's contract fulfilled its effects and thus became as if non-existent, so it is like an absolute claim of ownership. So much so that if they did not testify to his delivery, it is not dismissed. In al-Khulasa, payment of the price is not mentioned; only purchase with delivery is mentioned. In al-Bazzaziyya, after mentioning delivery, [it is said] it is like an absolute claim of ownership. He said: A group of our scholars said: It is also not dismissed, because the claim with purchase remains considered; for this reason the judge does not rule on separate increments, and sellers cannot reclaim from each other. If it were like an absolute claim of ownership, the matter would be contrary. The apparent [view] is what these [scholars] hold, due to the absolute [texts of] the matns regarding purchase. The author, may Allah have mercy on him, indicated by what he mentioned of the claim of an act and its rebuttal that the defendant, after a claim of an act against him, cannot transfer [the case] to another. So if he rebuts by saying it belongs to his young son after a claim of usurpation, it is not dismissed, as in al-Bazzaziyya; or if he rebuts by saying it is his father's property deposited with him, as in al-Khaniyya. His statement: 'And if the plaintiff says: I bought it from So-and-so, and the possessor says: So-and-so deposited it with me, the lawsuit falls' means without proof. The gist is that the plaintiff claimed ownership through a cause from the absentee, and the possessor rebutted by saying his hand [received it] from the absentee. Thus they agree that the original ownership belongs to the absentee. So its reaching the hand of the possessor is from him, so his hand is not a hand of contention. Unless the plaintiff produces evidence that So-and-so appointed him as agent for its receipt, because he proves by his evidence that he is more entitled to hold it. If the possessor affirms his purchase from him, the judge does not order delivery; it is not a judgment against the absentee by his admission, which is strange. He restricted [the ruling] to the receipt of the hand from the absentee to exclude the case where the possessor says: 'So-and-so's agent deposited it with me'; it is not dismissed without evidence, because he has not established receipt from the one to whom [the claim] goes. His statement: 'and it is strange' – I say: This issue was mentioned in the text at the end of the Book of Agency, before the chapter on dismissal of an agent. Its reasoning is that it is an admission against another, namely the owner of the deposit, so he does not hand it over to the claimant of agency for receipt or purchase, unlike the case where the absentee is in debt and a person claims agency for receipt and he affirms him, for then he pays him because debts are discharged by their equivalents. So it was an admission against himself, not against the absentee. So consider what the reason for wonder is. His statement: 'For it is not dismissed by the possessor's claim that that absentee deposited [it] in istihsan' – in Nur al-Ayn, the humble one says: Perhaps the aspect of istihsan is that usurpation is the removal of a rightful hand by establishing a wrongful hand, as mentioned in fiqh books. Thus the hand belongs to the usurper in the case of usurpation, unlike the case of theft, where the hand belongs to the possessor, because there is no hand for the thief legally. Moreover, the phrase 'no hand for the thief' is a subtle point whose beauty is not hidden from those with intellect. His statement: 'And if she claims theft, no' means it is not dismissed. Its apparent meaning is that she claimed theft by her brother, and it has recently been mentioned that if he claimed an act against someone other than the possessor, and the possessor rebutted with one of what we mentioned and provided proof, it is dismissed like an absolute claim of ownership. So his statement here is interpreted as that she claimed that it was stolen from her in the passive voice, making it a claim of an act against the possessor. If it is kept in its apparent meaning, it follows the opposite of the aforementioned istihsan.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explains the rule that when a plaintiff brings a claim, the defendant (possessor) can sometimes dismiss the lawsuit by proving that the property in his possession came from a specific third party (the absentee). The core principle is that if both parties agree that the property originally came from the same absentee, the defendant's possession is not adversarial (يد خصومة) against the plaintiff, and the case is dismissed. However, this rule has several conditions and exceptions, which the text elaborates through various scenarios. 

The text distinguishes between two types of claims: claims of an act (دعوى فعل) against the defendant (e.g., theft or usurpation) and claims of a contract (e.g., purchase from the defendant or from a third party). For claims of an act, if the defendant proves that the absentee deposited the item with him, and the plaintiff admits this, the case is dismissed, unless the plaintiff can prove that the absentee appointed the defendant as his agent. For claims of purchase from the defendant, the text discusses a difference of opinion: some scholars say it is dismissed like an absolute claim of ownership, while others (including a group of later Hanafi scholars) say it is not, because the claim of purchase remains valid and affects the rights of the seller.

A key distinction is between usurpation (غصب) and theft (سرقة). In istihsan (juristic preference), if the plaintiff claims usurpation by the absentee, the defendant's proof of deposit from that absentee dismisses the case, because the hand of the usurper is considered the effective hand. If the plaintiff claims theft by the absentee, the case is not dismissed, because the thief has no legal hand, so the defendant's possession is seen as independent. This distinction is applied to a case where a sister claims her brother took her property; if she claims usurpation, the defendant's proof of pledge (رهن) from the brother dismisses the case; if she claims theft, it does not.

Another distinction is between claims of bequest (وصية) and inheritance (وراثة). If the plaintiff claims property from a deceased person by bequest, and the defendant proves it is a deposit from that deceased, the case is dismissed until the heir or executor appears. But if the plaintiff claims by inheritance, the proof of deposit does not dismiss the case. 

Throughout, the text emphasizes that the absentee must be the same in both parties' claims. If the plaintiff claims from one absentee and the defendant proves deposit from a different absentee, the case is not dismissed.

Key Terms
إستحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; an exception to qiyas based on a stronger evidence or subtle analogy.
غصب (ghaṣb): Usurpation; wrongful but actual possession with the ability to exercise control.
سرقة (sariqa): Theft; taking property secretly with no legal recognition of possession.
وصية (waṣiyya): Bequest; a testamentary gift to a non-heir.
وراثة (wirātha): Inheritance; automatic transfer of ownership to heirs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A plaintiff claims his brother stole his watch. The defendant proves the brother deposited it. According to the istihsan discussed, what is the ruling?
A) The lawsuit is dismissed.
B) The lawsuit is not dismissed.
C) The lawsuit is dismissed only if the plaintiff admits the deposit.
D) The lawsuit is dismissed only if the claim is usurpation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses istihsan to distinguish theft from usurpation: proof of deposit dismisses a usurpation claim but not a theft claim, because a thief has no legal hand.

Judicial Determination of Death vs. Killing
Principle with illustrative cases and a dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that the day of death is not subject to judicial determination while the day of killing is, and apply it to evidentiary scenarios involving inheritance, marriage, and debts.

Core Idea
Day of death is not judicially fixed; day of killing is.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes a key procedural distinction in Hanafi qada: the date of a natural death is not considered legally fixed by a judicial ruling, whereas the date of a killing (murder) is. This affects the admissibility of contradictory evidence. The text presents several illustrative cases and notes a point of disagreement among later Hanafi scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
Read the first sentence: 'يوم الموت لا يدخل تحت القضاء ويوم القتل يدخل'. Understand that 'دخول تحت القضاء' means the event's date is legally fixed and cannot be contradicted by later evidence.
This is a rule of evidence, not a substantive fiqh ruling.
2
Analyze the first scenario (death)
If an heir proves death on a certain day, but a woman later proves marriage after that day, the marriage evidence is accepted because the death date is not judicially fixed.
The death date can be contradicted.
3
Analyze the second scenario (killing)
If an heir proves killing on a day, but a woman proves marriage after, the marriage evidence is rejected because the killing date is judicially fixed.
Contradictory evidence is inadmissible.
4
Extend the principle to other contracts
The text states 'وعلى هذا جميع العقود والمداينات' – the same rule applies to all contracts and debts.
Any date established by judicial determination (killing) cannot be contradicted; other dates are open.
5
Examine the dispute in al-Zahiriyya
Some scholars held that a defense based on a woman dying before the testator is valid; others rejected it, building on the principle that day of death is not subject to judicial determination.
This shows the principle can lead to ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن يوم الموت لا يدخل تحت القضاء ويوم القتل يدخل هكذا في العمادية والظهيرية والولوالجية والبزازية وغيرها. وفرعوا على الأول ما لو برهن الوارث على موت مورثه في يوم ثم برهنت امرأة على أن مورثه كان نكحها بعد ذلك اليوم يقضى لها بالنكاح، وعلى الثاني لو برهن الوارث على أنه قتل يوم كذا فبرهنت المرأة على أن هذا المقتول نكحها بعد ذلك اليوم لا يقبل، وعلى هذا جميع العقود والمداينات وكذا لو برهن الوارث على أن مورثه قتل يوم كذا فبرهن المدعى عليه أنه مات قبل هذا بزمان لا يسمع، ولو برهن على أن مورثه قتل يوم كذا فبرهن المدعى عليه أنه قتله فلان قبل هذا بزمان يكون دفعاً لدخوله تحت القضاء؛ هذه عبارة البزازية. وزاد الولوالجي موضحاً للثانية بقوله : ألا ترى أن امرأة لو أقامت البينة أنه يوم النحر بمكة فقضى بشهودها ثم أقامت أخرى بينة أنه تزوجها يوم النحر بخراسان لا تقبل بينة المرأة الأخرى لأن النكاح يدخل تحت القضاء فاعتبر ذلك التاريخ، فإذا ادعت امرأة أخرى . بعد ذلك التاريخ بتاريخ لم يقبل ا هـ . وفي الظهيرية ادعى ضيعة في يد رجل أنها كانت لفلان مات وتركها ميراثاً لفلانة لا وارث له غيرها، ثم إن فلانة ماتت وتركتها ميراثاً لي لا وارث لها غيري وقضى القاضي له بالضيعة فقال المقضي عليه دفعاً للدعوى إن فلانة التي تدعي أنت الإرث عنها لنفسك ماتت قبل فلان الذي تدعي الإرث عنه لفلانة اختلفوا فيه بعضهم قالوا إنه صحيح وبعضهم قالوا : إنه غير صحيح بناء على أن يوم الموت لا يدخل تحت القضاء ا.هـ.
Then know that the day of death is not subject to judicial determination, and the day of killing is subject to judicial determination; thus in al-Amadiyya, al-Zahiriyya, al-Walwalijiyya, al-Bazzaziyya, and others. And they derived from the first: if the heir proves the death of his testator on a day, then a woman proves that the testator married her after that day, judgment is given for her marriage. And on the second: if the heir proves that he was killed on such a day, and the woman proves that this killed person married her after that day, it is not accepted. And upon this are all contracts and debts. Similarly, if the heir proves that his testator was killed on such a day, and the defendant proves that he died before this by a time, it is not heard. And if he proves that his testator was killed on such a day, and the defendant proves that so-and-so killed him before this by a time, it would be a repulsion of its entry under the judgment. This is the wording of al-Bazzaziyya. And al-Walwaliji added clarifying the second by saying: Do you not see that if a woman establishes evidence that he was in Mecca on the day of Nahr, and judgment is given by her witnesses, then another woman establishes evidence that he married her on the day of Nahr in Khurasan, the evidence of the other woman is not accepted because marriage is subject to judicial determination, so that date is considered. So if another woman claims after that date with a date, it is not accepted. End quote. And in al-Zahiriyya: He claimed a property in the hand of a man that it belonged to so-and-so who died and left it as inheritance to so-and-so (a woman) with no other heir, then that woman died and left it as inheritance to me with no other heir, and the judge gave judgment for him of the property. Then the defendant said in defense of the claim: That woman whom you claim inheritance from for yourself died before the one from whom you claim inheritance for the woman. They differed about it: some said it is valid, some said it is not valid, based on the fact that the day of death is not subject to judicial determination. End quote.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage establishes an important principle in Hanafi procedural law: the day of a person's death is not considered a matter that can be established by judicial decree (لا يدخل تحت القضاء), meaning that evidence of events after the death can be accepted even if contradictory to the established death date. In contrast, the day of killing (e.g., murder) is subject to judicial determination (يدخل تحت القضاء), so evidence contradicting a known killing date is not accepted. The text gives examples: if an heir proves death on a certain day, then a woman proves the deceased married her after that day, her evidence is accepted because death day is not fixed judicially. But if the heir proves killing on a certain day, a woman's claim of marriage after that day is rejected. This extends to all contracts and debts. The Walwaliji adds an analogy: if a woman proves he was in Mecca on Eid al-Adha, and another proves marriage in Khurasan on the same day, the second is rejected because marriage is subject to judicial determination. The Zahiriyya case shows a dispute about whether a woman died before a man, with scholars differing based on this principle.

Key Terms
دخول تحت القضاء (dukhūl taḥt al-qaḍāʾ): The status of an event being subject to judicial determination, meaning its date is legally fixed and cannot be contradicted by later evidence.
يوم الموت (yawm al-mawt): The day of natural death, which is not subject to judicial determination per this principle.
يوم القتل (yawm al-qatl): The day of killing (murder), which is subject to judicial determination.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi principle, if an heir proves the deceased was killed on Monday, and a woman proves he married her on Tuesday, what happens?
A) The marriage evidence is accepted
B) The marriage evidence is rejected
C) The judge must reconcile both
D) The heir's evidence is rejected

Key Takeaway
The day of natural death is not judicially fixed, allowing contradictory evidence; the day of killing is judicially fixed, barring contradictory evidence.

Istihsan in Favor of Lineage Over Murder Evidence
Describing Abu Hanifa's istihsan in a case of conflicting evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conflicting evidence of lineage and murder, and explain how istihsan overrides strict qiyas.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa prioritizes lineage over murder evidence using istihsan, because preserving lineage outweighs strict analogy.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq shows how Abu Hanifa resolves a clash of evidence by applying istihsan (juristic preference) to uphold lineage, a primary objective of Shariah. Unlike strict qiyas that would reject the woman's evidence, istihsan accepts it to protect nasab. The ruling illustrates the Hanafi method of balancing proofs based on higher purposes, and it is agreed upon by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflict of evidence
A woman brings evidence that a man married her 15 years ago and fathered a child. The man's son brings evidence that the man was killed 20 years ago. Which claim should prevail?
2
Note the strict qiyas ruling
Under strict analogy (qiyas), the woman's evidence would be rejected because it contradicts the son's evidence about timing. The son's evidence would prevail, and the child would not be linked to the deceased.
3
Understand the istihsan reasoning
Abu Hanifa uses istihsan to accept the woman's evidence and establish the child's lineage. The basis is the principle of 'ihtiyat fi amr al-nasab' (preserving lineage), which is a recognized objective of Shariah that can override a strict analogy.
Istihsan is a valid source in the Hanafi school.
4
Distinguish the conditional scenario
If the woman proves only the marriage without the child, then the son's evidence prevails and he inherits. The istihsan applies only when the child's lineage is at stake.
This conditional distinction is agreed upon by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى باب دعوى الرجلين تسمعوقال عمر الحافظ : لا امرأة ومعها ولد وأقامت البينة أن والد هذا تزوجها منذ خمسة عشر سنة وأن هذا ولده منها ووارثه مع ابنه هذا قال أبو حنيفة : أستحسن في هذ أن أجيز بينة المرأة وأثبت نسب الولد ولا أبطل بينة الابن على القتل. وكان هذا الاستحسان للاحتياط في أمر النسب بدليل أنها لو أقامت البينة على النكاح ولم تأت بالولد فالبينة بينة الابن وله الميراث دون المرأة، وهذا قول أبي يوسف ومحمد ا هـ .
The book of claims, chapter of claims of two men. He said: 'Al-Hafiz Umar: No.' A woman with a child established evidence that this child's father married her fifteen years ago and that this child is from him and inherits with this son. Abu Hanifa said: I use istihsan in this to accept the woman's evidence and establish the lineage of the child, and I do not nullify the son's evidence regarding the murder. This istihsan was for precaution in the matter of lineage, with evidence that if she had established evidence of marriage and did not bring the child, then the evidence of the son is the evidence and he has the inheritance without the woman. This is the statement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling by Abu Hanifa where he uses istihsan (juristic preference) to resolve a conflict of evidence. A woman claims a man married her 15 years ago and that a child is from him, while the man's son claims the man was killed 20 years ago. Abu Hanifa prioritizes the woman's evidence for paternity over the son's evidence for murder, based on the principle of احتياط النسب (preserving lineage). The text then contrasts this with a scenario where the woman only proves marriage without the child: in that case, the son's evidence prevails. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad agree on this distinction. The logical proof (قياس) would reject the woman's evidence, but istihsan overrides it due to the higher objective of preserving lineage.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): A method of juristic preference where a ruling that is more in line with the objectives of Shariah is chosen over a strict analogy.
نسب (nasab): Lineage or paternity, which is to be preserved and established cautiously.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, usually testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a woman proves only marriage without a child, what happens to the son's evidence of murder?
A) The woman's evidence prevails, and the son's evidence is rejected.
B) The son's evidence prevails, and he inherits without the woman.
C) Both evidences are accepted, and the case is suspended.
D) The woman's evidence is accepted only if she also proves the child's paternity.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa uses istihsan to preserve lineage, overriding strict analogy, and this ruling is agreed upon by his two disciples.

Preferring More Recent Evidence in Hanafi Qada
Establishing a principle for preferring recent evidence over earlier when the fact is widely known

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle for preferring more recent evidence in court and identify the condition required for its application.

Core Idea
Recent well-known evidence overrides earlier contradictory claims in Hanafi qada.

In judicial proceedings, conflicting evidence about the timing of an event can arise. How does a Hanafi judge resolve such conflicts? This lesson draws on a passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq to explain Abu Hanifa's ruling: the more recent evidence is preferred, but only if the fact it proves is widely known (mashhur). This condition prevents misuse and ensures reliability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflict scenario
The passage presents a hypothetical: one party proves a killing a year ago, the other proves the victim led Friday prayer last week. The evidence conflicts in time.
This is a classic example of conflicting temporal evidence in qada.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
Abu Hanifa rules: 'Taking the more recent is more appropriate' – the judge should prefer the later event.
This is a principle of preference (taqdim) based on recency.
3
Identify the condition
Al-Ramli qualifies: the more recent evidence must concern a 'well-known' (mashhur) matter. The fact must be widely known among people.
Without mashhur, the principle may not apply; other factors could override.
4
Apply the example
If a man's death is well-known for twenty years, a claim of buying his house one year ago is rejected – the well-known death is more recent and preferred.
The well-known death is treated as recent knowledge even if the event itself is old.
5
Consider the implication
This principle ensures that widely accepted facts (mashhur) take precedence over individual claims, promoting consistency in judicial rulings.
It protects against fabricated evidence contradicting public knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : ولو أقام رجل البينة أنه قتل أبي منذ سنة وأقام المشهود عليه بينة أن أباه صلي بالناس الجمعة الماضية قال أبو حنيفة : الأخذ بالأحدث أولى إن كان شيئاً مشهوراً قال الرملي وهذا يقيد به ما مضى أيضاً وهذا قيد لازم لا بد منه حتى لو اشتهر موت رجل عند الناس فادعى رجل أنه اشترى منه داره منذ سنة وكان موته قد اشتهر عند الناس منذ عشرين سنة فدفعه بذلك يجب قبوله لما ذكر تأمل.
Then he said: If a man establishes evidence that he killed his father a year ago, and the one against whom the evidence is established brings evidence that his father led the people in Friday prayer last week, Abu Hanifa said: Taking the more recent is more appropriate if it is a well-known matter. Al-Ramli said: This qualifies what has passed as well. This is a necessary condition that must be present, so even if the death of a man is well-known among people, and a man claims that he bought a house from him a year ago, but his death has been well-known among people for twenty years, then he can rebut it with that, and it must be accepted because of what was mentioned; reflect.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage presents a principle from Abu Hanifa: when there is conflicting evidence about the time of an event, the more recent evidence is given precedence, provided that the event is well-known (مشهور) among people. The commentator al-Ramli adds a necessary condition: the fact must be widely known. For example, if it is well-known that a man died 20 years ago, and someone claims to have bought his house a year ago, the claim is rejected because the well-known death contradicts it. This principle is used to resolve contradictions in evidence by favoring what is more recent and reliably known.

Key Terms
الأحدث (al-ahdath): The more recent event or evidence in time.
مشهور (mashhur): Well-known, commonly known among people to the extent that it is indisputable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition does al-Ramli mention for the principle of 'taking the more recent' to apply?
A) The evidence must come from two male witnesses.
B) The fact must be well-known (مشهور) among people.
C) The earlier evidence must be proven false.
D) The parties must agree to accept it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi judge gives precedence to the more recent evidence only if the fact it proves is widely known (mashhur).

6 min
Evidence of One Killer for Two Murders
Stating that if one killer is accused of two murders on the same day in different places, the evidence is not accepted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the admissibility of evidence when one killer is accused of two simultaneous murders and explain the basis of impossibility.

Core Idea
One person cannot be in two places at once — evidence of two simultaneous killings by the same killer is impossible and therefore rejected.

In cases of murder, evidence must be plausible. The Hanafi school recognizes that if a single individual is accused of killing two different people in different locations at the same time, the claim defies physical possibility. The evidence is therefore not accepted, preserving the integrity of judicial proceedings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage describes a situation where one killer (qatil) is accused of killing two victims (maqtulayn) on the same day in different places, such as Mecca and Kufa on the day of Nahr.
2
State the ruling
The ruling is that the evidence is not accepted (lam tuqbal). The court rejects the claim outright.
3
Understand the reasoning
The basis for rejection is physical impossibility. One person cannot be present simultaneously in two distant locations to commit both murders. The claim is inherently improbable.
4
Note the source
This ruling is attributed to Imam Muhammad, as recorded in Nawadir Ibn Rustom. It is a relied-upon transmission within the Hanafi school.
Nawadir are rare transmissions, but this one is cited without objection.
5
Distinguish from other cases
If there are two killers, each accused of one murder, each case is heard separately and half the blood-money is awarded. The impossibility applies only when the killer is the same.
The preceding sentence in the source text deals with that scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما لو كان القاتل واحداً والمقتول اثنين لم تقبل؛ ذكره في نوادر ابن رستم عن محمد ا .
But if the killer is one and the killed are two, it is not accepted; mentioned in Nawadir Ibn Rustom from Muhammad.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This is a short ruling attributed to Imam Muhammad: if one person is accused of killing two different people on the same day in different places (e.g., Mecca and Kufa on the day of Nahr), the evidence is not accepted because it is physically impossible for one person to be in two places at once. The previous sentence mentioned that if two different killers are accused of killing two different victims, each case is heard and half the blood-money is awarded. But if the killer is the same, the evidence is rejected due to impossibility.

Key Terms
قاتل (qatil): Killer, the person who committed the murder.
مقتول (maqtul): Victim of murder.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the evidence not accepted when one killer is accused of killing two people on the same day in different places?
A) Because the witnesses are not reliable.
B) Because it is impossible for one person to be in two places at once.
C) Because the blood-money would be doubled.
D) Because the court only accepts evidence from the victim's relatives.

Key Takeaway
Evidence of a single person killing two victims at the same time in different places is rejected due to physical impossibility, according to the Hanafi school.

Priority of Dated Evidence for the Possessor
Intra-Hanafi dispute on preferring possessor's dated evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for preferring the possessor's dated evidence over the external claimant's, and identify the intra-madhhab difference regarding its basis.

Core Idea
Possessor's earlier dated evidence implies prior ownership; any later claim must derive from him.

In property disputes, both parties may present evidence with dates. The Hanafi school gives special weight to the possessor's evidence if its date is earlier. This lesson examines the reasoning behind this preference, including an internal debate between Abu Hanifa's companions and a narration from Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases
The passage lists three scenarios where the possessor's evidence prevails. Focus on the first: both present dated evidence, and the possessor's date is earlier.
Other cases involve birth or non-repeating cause, and purchase from the external claimant.
2
Understand the Sahibayn's argument
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that a dated evidence implies transfer: if ownership is established for someone at an earlier time, a later claim must be based on transfer from that person.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad initially held that both evidences are asābah (absolute) and dates are irrelevant; but he later returned to the Sahibayn's view.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Under the preferred view, the possessor wins. Under the opposing narration, the external claimant would win if both evidences are absolute and dates are ignored.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو) برهن الخارج على ملك مؤرخ وتاريخ ذي اليد أسبق أو برهنا على النتاج أو ملك لا يتكرر أو الخارج على الملك وذو اليد على الشراء منه فذو اليد أحق) بيان لثلاث مسائل تقدم فيها بينة ذي اليد على الخارج : الأولى برهنا على ملك مؤرخ وسبق تاريخ ذي اليد وهذا عندهما، ورواية عن محمد وعنه عدم قبولها رجع إليه لأن البينتين قامتا على مطلق الملك ولم يتعرضا لجهة الملك فكان التقدم والتأخر سواء ولهما أن البينة مع التاريخ متضمنة معنى الدفع فإن الملك إذا ثبت لشخص في وقت فثبوته لغيره بعده لا يكون إلا بالتلقي من جهته وبينة ذي اليد على الدفع مقبولة وعلى هذا الخلاف لو كانت الدار في أيديهما والمعنى ما بينا. قيد بسبق تاريخ ذي اليد لأنه لو لم يكن لهما تاريخ أو استوى تاريخهما أو أرخت إحداهما فقط كان الخارج أولى، وكذا لو كانت في أيديهما فإنها تقدم المؤقتة على غيرها
His saying: (And if) the external claimant presents dated evidence of ownership and the date of the possessor is earlier, or both present evidence of birth or of a non-repeating cause [of ownership], or the external claimant [presents evidence] of absolute ownership and the possessor [presents evidence] of purchase from him, then the possessor is more deserving. This is an explanation of three cases in which the evidence of the possessor prevails over that of the external claimant: The first case: both present dated evidence of ownership and the date of the possessor is earlier. This is the position of the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], and there is a narration from Muhammad that he returned to not accepting it, because both pieces of evidence pertain to absolute ownership and do not address the mode of acquisition, so precedence and lateness are equal. Their argument is that evidence with a date inherently implies the meaning of transfer, for if ownership is established for a person at a time, then its establishment for another after that can only be through acquisition from him, and the evidence of the possessor regarding transfer is acceptable. Upon this difference, if the house were in the hands of both, the meaning is as we have set forth. He restricted it to the precedence of the possessor's date because if neither had a date, or their dates were equal, or only one of them was dated, then the external claimant is more deserving, and likewise if it were in the hands of both, the dated evidence is preferred over the undated.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the preference of evidence when both parties present dated ownership claims and the possessor's date is earlier. The ruling from the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) is that the possessor is preferred because a dated evidence inherently implies that the ownership was transferred from the earlier owner. In a narration, Imam Muhammad initially held that both evidences are equal since they don't specify the mode of acquisition, but he later returned to the Sahibayn's view. The text also notes exceptions: if dates are missing, equal, or only one is dated, the external claimant prevails.

Key Terms
البينة المؤرخة (al-bayyinah al-mu'arrakhah): Evidence that includes a date specifying when the ownership was established.
ذو اليد (dhu al-yad): The person currently in possession of the property.
الخارج (al-kharij): The claimant who is not in possession, i.e., the external claimant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the external claimant and the possessor both present dated evidence and the possessor's date is earlier, what is the ruling according to the Sahibayn?
A) External claimant wins
B) Possessor wins
C) The judge decides based on other factors
D) Both are rejected

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, possessor's earlier dated evidence takes priority because it implies prior ownership and subsequent transfer.

6 min
Preference of the Possessor in Birth Evidence
Ruling that possessor's birth evidence prevails over external claimant's

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when both the possessor and external claimant present evidence of birth (nataj) and identify the hadith proof that supports the possessor's preference.

Core Idea
When birth evidence is equal, the possessor's physical possession tips the scale.

In disputes over livestock ownership, both parties may claim the animal was born from their own herd. When each presents such evidence, the Hanafi school gives priority to the current possessor, based on a clear prophetic precedent. This lesson examines the ruling and its proof from the hadith of Jabir ibn Abdullah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting claims
Both the external claimant and the possessor present evidence (baiyinah) that the animal was born from their own livestock. The evidence is equal in kind.
The term 'nataj' refers specifically to evidence of birth, not other proofs of ownership.
2
Apply the principle of possession
Since both evidences are equal, the possessor's physical possession (yad) gives additional weight to his evidence, making him preferable.
Possession is a presumption of ownership that remains until overcome by superior evidence.
3
Locate the prophetic precedent
The hadith of Jabir ibn Abdullah reports that the Prophet (peace be upon him) judged similarly, giving the camel to the possessor when both brought birth evidence.
This hadith is cited as authentic (sahih) and well-known (mashhur).
4
Understand the exception
Ordinarily, evidence (baiyinah) alone decides. But when evidence pertains to birth (which possession does not indicate), possession becomes the tiebreaker. This case is made an exception (makhsusah) due to the hadith.
The exception is limited to birth evidence; other types of evidence may follow the general rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية أقام كل من الخارج وذي اليد بينة على النتاج فصاحب اليد أولى لأن البينة قامت على ما لا يدل عليه اليد فاستويا وترجحت بينة ذي اليد باليد فيقضى له وهذا هو الصحيح، ودليله من السنة ما روى جابر بن عبد الله أن رجلاً ادعى ناقة في يد رجل وأقام البينة أنها ناقته نتجت وأقام الذي في يديه البينة أنها ناقته نتجها فقضى بها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم للذي هي في يده، وهذا حديث صحيح مشهور فصارت مسألة النتاج مخصوصة؛ كذا في المحيط.
The second [case]: Each of the external claimant and the possessor presented evidence of birth (نتاج). Then the possessor is more deserving, because the evidence pertained to something that the hand (possession) does not indicate, so they were equal, and the evidence of the possessor was given weight by virtue of the hand, so judgment is given for him, and this is the correct [view]. Its proof from the Sunnah is what Jabir ibn Abdullah narrated: that a man claimed a she-camel in the hand of another man, and presented evidence that it was his camel born from his livestock, and the one in whose hand it was presented evidence that it was his camel born from his livestock, so the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) gave judgment in favor of the one in whose hand it was. This is an authentic and well-known hadith, thus the case of birth (نتاج) became an exception; as in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage states the ruling for when both the external claimant and the possessor present evidence that the animal was born from their own livestock. Since the evidence is equal, the possessor's physical possession tips the scale. The proof is a hadith from Jabir ibn Abdullah where the Prophet judged in favor of the possessor. This is considered an exception to the general rule that evidence alone should decide; here possession adds weight. The text confirms this as the correct (صحيح) position.

Key Terms
النتاج (al-nataj): Evidence that the animal was born from one's own livestock.
ذو اليد (dhu al-yad): The person currently in possession of the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Upon what basis did the Prophet (peace be upon him) rule in the case of the camel when both parties provided evidence of birth?
A) The earlier evidence
B) The possessor's evidence
C) External claimant's evidence
D) They split equally

Key Takeaway
When both the possessor and an external claimant present equal evidence of birth (nataj), the possessor is preferred based on the hadith of Jabir.

8 min
Preference for the Possessor in Claims of Birth (Nataj)
Setting the general rule for preference in birth claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for preferring the possessor's evidence in nataj claims and identify the exception when the mother's absolute ownership is disputed.

Core Idea
The possessor's birth evidence is preferred; but if the mother's ownership is disputed, the external claimant wins.

When both the possessor and an external claimant bring evidence that an animal or slave was born in their ownership, Hanafi law has nuanced rules. This lesson explores the general principle and its key exception, drawing from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq's commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Guided Path
1
Define Nataj
Identify what nataj means: birth of an animal or any non-repetitive creation like weaving cloth. Understand that evidence of nataj claims original ownership from the beginning.
2
Identify the General Preference Rule
When both parties prove nataj in their own or their seller's ownership, the possessor (dhu al-yad) wins. The possessor represents his seller, so his evidence prevails.
3
Recognize the Condition on Testimony
Testimony must establish ownership, not just lineage or birth. Merely testifying that a child was born to the slave-woman without stating ownership is insufficient.
From Khizanat al-Akmal: evidence of birth without ownership is not accepted.
4
Note the Exception: Disputed Mother
If the external claimant proves absolute ownership of the mother (and thus the child as dependent), and the possessor also claims, the external claimant wins. The possessor's preference only holds when the mother's ownership is not disputed.
This is a key nuance from Jami' al-Fusoolayn.
5
Apply to a Building Case
Similar rule applies to inheritance: if both claim a house was built by their father long ago, the possessor's evidence is preferred.
From al-Qunyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى باب دعوى الرجلين
نصفان في رواية؛ كذا في جامع الفصولين والنتاج ولادة الحيوان ووضعه عنده من نتجت عنده بالبناء للمفعول ولدت ووضعت كما في المغرب. والمراد ولادته في ملك ملكه أو في بائعه أو مورثه ولذا قال في خزانة الأكمل : لو أقام بينة أن هذه الدابة نتجت عنده أو نسج هذا الثوب عنده أو أن هذا الولد ولدته أمته ولم يشهدوا بالملك له فإنه لا يقضى له اهـ. وكذا لو شهدوا أنها بنت أمته لأنهم إنما شهدوا بالنسب؛ كذا في الخزانة. وإنما قلت أو ملك بائعه لما في جامع الفصولين : برهن كل من الخارج وذي اليد على نتاج في ملك بائعه حكم لذي اليد إذ كل منهما خصم عن بائعه فكان بائعهما حضر أو ادعيا ملكاً بنتاج فإنه يحكم لذي اليد، ولو برهن أنه له ولد في ملكه وبرهن ذو اليد أنه له ولد في ملك بائعه حكم به لذي اليد لأنه خصم عمن تلقى الملك منه ويده يد المتلقي منه فكأنه حضر وبرهن على النتاج والمدعي في يده يحكم له به كذا هذا .اهـ. و به ظهر أنه لا يترجج نتاج في ملكه على نتاج في ملك بائعه، ولا يشترط أن يشهدوا بأن أمه في ملكه لكن لو شهدت بينة بذلك دون أخرى قدمت عليها لما في الخزانة : عبد في يد رجل أقام رجل البينة أنه عبده ولد في ملكه وأقام آخر البينة أنه عبده ولد في ملكه أمته هذه قضى للذي أمه في يده، فإن أقام صاحب اليد من البينة أنه عبده ولد في ملكه من أمة أخرى فصاحب اليد أولى عبد في يد رجل أقام رجل البينة أنه عبده ولد من أمته هذه من عبده هذا وأقام رجل آخر البيئة بمثل ذلك فهو بينهما نصفان فيكون ابن عبدين ،وأمتين وقال صاحباه لا يثبت نسبه منهما اهـ. وفي جامع الفصولين: برهن الخارج أن هذه أمته ولدت هذا القن في ملكي وبرهن ذو اليد على مثله يحكم بها للمدعي لأنهما ادعيا في الأمة ملكاً مطلقاً فيقضى بها للمدعي ثم يستحق القن تبعاً اهـ. وبهذا ظهر أن ذا اليد إنما يقدم في دعوى النتاج على الخارج أن لو لم يتنازعا في الأم، أما لو تنازعا فيها في الملك المطلق وشهدوا به وبنتاج ولدها فإنه لا يقدم وهذه يجب حفظها. وإنما قلت أو ملك مورثة لما في القنية كما تقدم بينة ذي اليد إذا ادعى أولية الملك بالنتاج عنده فكذا إذا ادعاه عند مورثه فإذا أقاما بينة على عمارة دار أن أباه بناها منذ ستين سنة وقالا مات وتركها ميراثاً لنا فبينة ذي اليد أولى اهـ.
Book of Claims, Chapter on Claims of Two Men: Half each in a narration; as in Jami' al-Fusoolayn. An-Nataj (birth) means the giving birth of an animal and its placement with him; from 'nutihat 'indahu' in the passive voice means she gave birth and delivered, as in al-Mughrib. What is meant is its birth in his ownership, or in the ownership of his seller or his testator. Hence it is said in Khizanat al-Akmal: If he brings evidence that this animal was born with him, or that this cloth was woven with him, or that this child was born to his slave-woman, and they did not testify to ownership for him, then it is not judged for him. Likewise if they testify that she is the daughter of his slave-woman because they only testified to lineage; as in al-Khizanah. I only said 'or the ownership of his seller' because in Jami' al-Fusoolayn: If each of the external claimant and the possessor proves birth in the ownership of his seller, judgment is given to the possessor since each of them is a litigant on behalf of his seller, so it is as if their sellers were present. Or if they both claim ownership by birth, then judgment is given to the possessor. And if he proves that he has a child in his ownership and the possessor proves that he has a child in the ownership of his seller, judgment is given to the possessor because he is a litigant on behalf of the one from whom he received ownership, and his hand is the hand of the one from whom he received, so it is as if he was present and proved the birth, and the claimant is in his hand, so judgment is given to him. From this it appears that birth in his ownership is not given preference over birth in the ownership of his seller. It is not a condition that they testify that his mother is in his ownership, but if one evidence testifies to that without the other, it is given precedence over it. As in al-Khizanah: A slave in the hand of a man; a man brings evidence that he is his slave, born in his ownership, and another brings evidence that he is his slave, born in the ownership of his slave-woman; judgment is given to the one whose mother is in his hand. If the possessor brings evidence that he is his slave, born in his ownership from another slave-woman, then the possessor is more entitled. A slave in the hand of a man; a man brings evidence that he is his slave, born from this his slave-woman from this his male slave, and another man brings evidence like that; then it is half between them, so he is the son of two male slaves and two female slaves. His two companions said: His lineage is not established from them. In Jami' al-Fusoolayn: If the external claimant proves that this is his slave-woman who gave birth to this slave in my ownership, and the possessor proves the like, judgment is given to the claimant because they both claimed absolute ownership in the slave-woman, so it is judged for the claimant, then the slave is acquired as a dependent. Thus it appears that the possessor is given preference in a claim of birth over the external claimant only if they do not dispute the mother. If they dispute her in absolute ownership and testify to that and to the birth of her child, then he is not given preference. This must be memorized. I only said 'or the ownership of his testator' because in al-Qunyah: As previously stated, the evidence of the possessor when he claims original ownership by birth with him, so likewise if he claims it with his testator. So if they both bring evidence regarding a building of a house that his father built it sixty years ago and he died leaving it as inheritance for us, then the evidence of the possessor is preferred.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addressing the rules of evidence when both parties claim ownership by birth (nataj). The author first defines nataj as the birth of an animal or any non-repetitive production like weaving cloth or the birth of a child from a slave-woman. The key principle is that the possessor's evidence of birth is generally preferred, but there are nuances. If both parties bring evidence that the animal was born in their respective ownership, the possessor wins because his hand supports his claim. Even if the external claimant proves birth in his own ownership and the possessor proves birth in his seller's ownership, the possessor still prevails, as he represents his seller's claim. However, if the ownership of the mother is disputed absolutely (i.e., who owns the mother), then the external claimant's evidence takes precedence because the claim involves absolute ownership of the mother, not just the offspring. The passage also includes an intra-Hanafi dispute: if evidence is brought that a slave is the son of two male slaves and two female slaves, Abu Hanifa said the slave is divided equally between them, but his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said lineage is not established from both fathers. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) opinion in the Hanafi school is that the possessor's evidence is preferred in birth claims as long as the mother's ownership is not disputed. This is from the zahir al-riwayah.

Key Terms
نتاج (nataj): Birth or production of an animal; by extension, any non-repetitive creation (like weaving a cloth that is only woven once).
ذو اليد (dhu al-yad): The person who currently holds the property in his hand (possession).
خارج (kharaj): The external claimant who does not have possession.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically consisting of two male witnesses or one male and two females.
أولية الملك (awwaliyyat al-mulk): Original ownership; that the property was originally owned by this person from the beginning (e.g., by birth).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over a slave, the external claimant brings evidence that the slave was born in his ownership. The possessor brings evidence that the slave was born in the ownership of his seller. Who gets the slave?
A) The external claimant
B) The possessor
C) The slave is divided equally
D) The judge appoints an expert

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the possessor's evidence of birth is generally preferred, but if the mother's absolute ownership is proven by the external claimant, he wins instead.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Slave Claimed as Son of Two Fathers
Intra-Hanafi dispute on evidence of dual paternity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding a slave claimed as the son of two fathers and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa divides the slave equally; Sahibayn reject dual paternity and uphold the possessor.

This lesson examines a specific case from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq where two external claimants both bring evidence that a slave in the hand of a third party is the son of their respective male and female slaves. The Hanafi imams disagreed on the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Scenario
A slave is in the hand of a man. Two external claimants each present witnesses that the slave was born from their own slave-woman and their own male slave.
The slave is the object of dispute, not a claimant.
2
State Abu Hanifa's View
Abu Hanifa ruled that the slave is divided equally (half each) between the two claimants, because both pieces of evidence are equally strong in establishing birth ownership.
He treats the slave as property, not lineage.
3
State the Sahibayn's View
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani said that the slave's lineage is not established from both fathers, so the possessor (the person whose hand the slave is in) retains the slave entirely.
They focus on the impossibility of dual biological paternity.
4
Analyze the Reasoning
The disagreement hinges on whether the evidence of birth (nataj) is about property or lineage. Abu Hanifa sees it as a property claim; the Sahibayn see lineage as a biological fact that cannot be shared.
5
Identify the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) opinion in the Hanafi school is that of the Sahibayn, as it avoids the absurdity of dual paternity and aligns with caution.
From the tarjih authority in the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى باب دعوى الرجلين
نصفان في رواية؛ كذا في جامع الفصولين والنتاج ولادة الحيوان ووضعه عنده من نتجت عنده بالبناء للمفعول ولدت ووضعت كما في المغرب. والمراد ولادته في ملك ملكه أو في بائعه أو مورثه ولذا قال في خزانة الأكمل : لو أقام بينة أن هذه الدابة نتجت عنده أو نسج هذا الثوب عنده أو أن هذا الولد ولدته أمته ولم يشهدوا بالملك له فإنه لا يقضى له اهـ. وكذا لو شهدوا أنها بنت أمته لأنهم إنما شهدوا بالنسب؛ كذا في الخزانة. وإنما قلت أو ملك بائعه لما في جامع الفصولين : برهن كل من الخارج وذي اليد على نتاج في ملك بائعه حكم لذي اليد إذ كل منهما خصم عن بائعه فكان بائعهما حضر أو ادعيا ملكاً بنتاج فإنه يحكم لذي اليد، ولو برهن أنه له ولد في ملكه وبرهن ذو اليد أنه له ولد في ملك بائعه حكم به لذي اليد لأنه خصم عمن تلقى الملك منه ويده يد المتلقي منه فكأنه حضر وبرهن على النتاج والمدعي في يده يحكم له به كذا هذا .اهـ. و به ظهر أنه لا يترجج نتاج في ملكه على نتاج في ملك بائعه، ولا يشترط أن يشهدوا بأن أمه في ملكه لكن لو شهدت بينة بذلك دون أخرى قدمت عليها لما في الخزانة : عبد في يد رجل أقام رجل البينة أنه عبده ولد في ملكه وأقام آخر البينة أنه عبده ولد في ملكه أمته هذه قضى للذي أمه في يده، فإن أقام صاحب اليد من البينة أنه عبده ولد في ملكه من أمة أخرى فصاحب اليد أولى عبد في يد رجل أقام رجل البينة أنه عبده ولد من أمته هذه من عبده هذا وأقام رجل آخر البيئة بمثل ذلك فهو بينهما نصفان فيكون ابن عبدين ،وأمتين وقال صاحباه لا يثبت نسبه منهما اهـ. وفي جامع الفصولين: برهن الخارج أن هذه أمته ولدت هذا القن في ملكي وبرهن ذو اليد على مثله يحكم بها للمدعي لأنهما ادعيا في الأمة ملكاً مطلقاً فيقضى بها للمدعي ثم يستحق القن تبعاً اهـ. وبهذا ظهر أن ذا اليد إنما يقدم في دعوى النتاج على الخارج أن لو لم يتنازعا في الأم، أما لو تنازعا فيها في الملك المطلق وشهدوا به وبنتاج ولدها فإنه لا يقدم وهذه يجب حفظها. وإنما قلت أو ملك مورثة لما في القنية كما تقدم بينة ذي اليد إذا ادعى أولية الملك بالنتاج عنده فكذا إذا ادعاه عند مورثه فإذا أقاما بينة على عمارة دار أن أباه بناها منذ ستين سنة وقالا مات وتركها ميراثاً لنا فبينة ذي اليد أولى اهـ.
Book of Claims, Chapter on Claims of Two Men: Half each in a narration; as in Jami' al-Fusoolayn. An-Nataj (birth) means the giving birth of an animal and its placement with him; from 'nutihat 'indahu' in the passive voice means she gave birth and delivered, as in al-Mughrib. What is meant is its birth in his ownership, or in the ownership of his seller or his testator. Hence it is said in Khizanat al-Akmal: If he brings evidence that this animal was born with him, or that this cloth was woven with him, or that this child was born to his slave-woman, and they did not testify to ownership for him, then it is not judged for him. Likewise if they testify that she is the daughter of his slave-woman because they only testified to lineage; as in al-Khizanah. I only said 'or the ownership of his seller' because in Jami' al-Fusoolayn: If each of the external claimant and the possessor proves birth in the ownership of his seller, judgment is given to the possessor since each of them is a litigant on behalf of his seller, so it is as if their sellers were present. Or if they both claim ownership by birth, then judgment is given to the possessor. And if he proves that he has a child in his ownership and the possessor proves that he has a child in the ownership of his seller, judgment is given to the possessor because he is a litigant on behalf of the one from whom he received ownership, and his hand is the hand of the one from whom he received, so it is as if he was present and proved the birth, and the claimant is in his hand, so judgment is given to him. From this it appears that birth in his ownership is not given preference over birth in the ownership of his seller. It is not a condition that they testify that his mother is in his ownership, but if one evidence testifies to that without the other, it is given precedence over it. As in al-Khizanah: A slave in the hand of a man; a man brings evidence that he is his slave, born in his ownership, and another brings evidence that he is his slave, born in the ownership of his slave-woman; judgment is given to the one whose mother is in his hand. If the possessor brings evidence that he is his slave, born in his ownership from another slave-woman, then the possessor is more entitled. A slave in the hand of a man; a man brings evidence that he is his slave, born from this his slave-woman from this his male slave, and another man brings evidence like that; then it is half between them, so he is the son of two male slaves and two female slaves. His two companions said: His lineage is not established from them. In Jami' al-Fusoolayn: If the external claimant proves that this is his slave-woman who gave birth to this slave in my ownership, and the possessor proves the like, judgment is given to the claimant because they both claimed absolute ownership in the slave-woman, so it is judged for the claimant, then the slave is acquired as a dependent. Thus it appears that the possessor is given preference in a claim of birth over the external claimant only if they do not dispute the mother. If they dispute her in absolute ownership and testify to that and to the birth of her child, then he is not given preference. This must be memorized. I only said 'or the ownership of his testator' because in al-Qunyah: As previously stated, the evidence of the possessor when he claims original ownership by birth with him, so likewise if he claims it with his testator. So if they both bring evidence regarding a building of a house that his father built it sixty years ago and he died leaving it as inheritance for us, then the evidence of the possessor is preferred.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addressing the rules of evidence when both parties claim ownership by birth (nataj). The author first defines nataj as the birth of an animal or any non-repetitive production like weaving cloth or the birth of a child from a slave-woman. The key principle is that the possessor's evidence of birth is generally preferred, but there are nuances. If both parties bring evidence that the animal was born in their respective ownership, the possessor wins because his hand supports his claim. Even if the external claimant proves birth in his own ownership and the possessor proves birth in his seller's ownership, the possessor still prevails, as he represents his seller's claim. However, if the ownership of the mother is disputed absolutely (i.e., who owns the mother), then the external claimant's evidence takes precedence because the claim involves absolute ownership of the mother, not just the offspring. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) opinion in the Hanafi school is that the possessor's evidence is preferred in birth claims as long as the mother's ownership is not disputed. This is from the zahir al-riwayah.

ذو اليد (dhu al-yad): The person who currently holds the property in his hand (possession).

External Claimant's Transaction Evidence vs. Possessor's Birth: Preference Rule
Procedural rule on preference of evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when an external claimant proves a specific transaction (ghasb, ijara, ariya, etc.) against the possessor, his evidence is preferred over the possessor's evidence of birth.

Core Idea
External claimant's transaction evidence beats possessor's birth evidence—it proves a direct, unrefuted act.

In the previous lesson, we learned that when both parties claim ownership by birth, the possessor's evidence is preferred. However, this rule has a critical exception: if the external claimant presents evidence showing that the property came into the possessor's hand through a specific transaction—such as lease, loan, or usurpation—then the external claimant's evidence takes precedence. This passage from al-Muhit explains the rationale and extends the rule to cases involving emancipation, tadbir, and istilad. Understanding this exception is essential for correctly weighing evidence in property disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties' claims
Determine what each party is proving: the external claimant alleges a specific transaction (ghasb, ijara, ariya, i'taq, tadbir, istilad) against the possessor; the possessor claims birth (the property was born in his ownership).
The possessor's claim of birth is a claim of original ownership without any encumbrance.
2
Classify the external claimant's evidence
Note that the external claimant's evidence establishes an act (the transaction) that directly involves the possessor. This act is the basis for the external claimant's right to reclaim the property.
The act may be a tort (ghasb) or a contract (ijara, ariya).
3
Apply the preference rule
The external claimant's evidence is preferred because it proves the possessor's possession is not original but derived from the transaction. The possessor's evidence of birth does not refute the transaction; it only proves birth, which is consistent with the external claimant's earlier ownership.
The key is that the external claimant's evidence is 'more establishing' (akthar ithbātan).
4
Handle variations with emancipation and similar rights
If the external claimant also claims emancipation, tadbir, or istilad along with birth, his evidence is even stronger because it proves original ownership without any claim against it. Conversely, if the external claimant claims emancipation alone without birth, the possessor's birth evidence may prevail if the possessor also claims emancipation in a stronger form.
The strength of the evidence depends on how it establishes original ownership and freedom from counterclaims.
5
Test with scenarios from the text
Work through the examples: external claimant claims ghasb vs. possessor claims birth → external claimant wins. External claimant claims i'taq with birth vs. possessor claims birth alone → external claimant wins. External claimant claims tadbir/istilad with birth vs. possessor claims absolute emancipation with birth → possessor wins (if stronger claim). External claimant claims absolute emancipation with birth vs. possessor claims tadbir/istilad with birth → external claimant wins.
The principle is that the evidence which proves ownership in a way that no claim can be imagined against it is stronger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بكون كل منهما مدعياً للملك والنتاج فقط إذ لو ادعى الخارج الفعل على ذي اليد كالغصب والإجارة والعارية فبينة الخارج أولى، وإن ادعى ذو اليد النتاج لأن بينة الخارج في هذه الصور أكثر إثباتاً لإثباتها الفعل على ذي اليد إذ هو غير ثابت أصلاً كما ذكره الشارح. وفي المحيط : الخارج وذو اليد إذا أقاما البينة على نتاج العبد والخارج يدعي الإعتاق أيضاً فهو أولى، وكذا إذا ادعيا وهو في يد ثالث وأحدهما يدعي الإعتاق فهو أولى لأن بينة النتاج مع العتق أكثر إثباتاً لأنها أثبتت أولية الملك على وجه لا يستحق عليه أصلاً، وبينة ذي اليد أثبتت الملك على وجه يتصور استحقاق ذلك عليه بخلاف ما إذا ادعى الخارج العتق مع مطلق الملك وذو اليد ادعى النتاج فبينة ذي اليد أولى لأنهما لم يستويا في إثبات أولية الملك لأن الخارج أثبت الملك فلم يعتبر العتق للترجيح. وكذا لو ادعى الخارج التدبير أو الاستيلاد مع النتاج أيضاً وذو اليد مع النتاج عتقاً باتاً فهو أولى، ولو ادعى ذو اليد التدبير أو الاستيلاد مع النتاج والخارج ادعى عتقاً باتاً مع النتاج فالخارج أولى اهـ.
And it is conditioned that each of them is only claiming ownership and birth; because if the external claimant claims an act (like usurpation, lease, or loan) against the possessor, then the external claimant's evidence is preferred, even if the possessor claims birth, because the external claimant's evidence in these cases establishes more: it proves the act against the possessor, which was not established at all, as the commentator mentioned. In al-Muhit: If the external claimant and the possessor both bring evidence of the birth of the slave, and the external claimant also claims emancipation, then his evidence is preferred. Likewise if both claim while it is in the hand of a third party, and one of them claims emancipation, then he is preferred because the evidence of birth together with emancipation establishes more: it proves original ownership in a way that no claim can be made against it, while the possessor's evidence proves ownership in a way that a claim can be imagined against it. Unlike if the external claimant claims emancipation with absolute ownership, and the possessor claims birth, then the possessor's evidence is preferred because they are not equal in establishing original ownership: the external claimant proved ownership, so emancipation is not considered for preference. Likewise if the external claimant claims tadbir or istilad together with birth, and the possessor claims birth together with absolute emancipation, then he is preferred. If the possessor claims tadbir or istilad with birth, and the external claimant claims absolute emancipation with birth, then the external claimant is preferred.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage expands on a condition of the previous rule: the preference for the possessor's evidence of birth only applies when both parties are merely claiming ownership by birth. If the external claimant instead claims that the property came into the possessor's hand through a specific transaction (like lease, loan for use, or pawn), then the external claimant's evidence takes precedence. The reason is that the external claimant's evidence establishes a direct act against the possessor (the transaction), which the possessor's evidence of birth does not refute. The passage then illustrates with cases from al-Muhit involving emancipation, tadbir, and istilad (motherhood of a child). The key is that the evidence which establishes the stronger claim (original ownership with no encumbrances) is preferred. The mufta bihi position is that when the external claimant brings evidence of a transaction, his evidence prevails over the possessor's evidence of birth.

Key Terms
إجارة (ijara): Lease or hire contract.
عارية (ariya): Loan for use, gratuitous loan.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation; wrongful taking.
إعتاق (i'taq): Emancipation of a slave.
تدبير (tadbir): A contract where a slave is freed upon the master's death.
استيلاد (istilad): A slave-woman becoming umm walad by bearing her master's child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The possessor's evidence
B) The external claimant's evidence
C) Both are equal, so the property is divided
D) The evidence of birth is always preferred

Key Takeaway
External claimant's evidence of a transaction (ghasb, ijara, ariya, i'taq, tadbir, istilad) is preferred over possessor's birth evidence because it proves a direct act and establishes stronger original ownership.

Non-Repetitive Causes: Analogy to Birth in Evidence
Defining which production acts are analogous to birth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that distinguishes non-repetitive from repetitive causes of production to determine which party's evidence is preferred in a property dispute.

Core Idea
Non-repetitive production = birth analogy, possessor wins; repetitive = absolute ownership, external claimant wins.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a niche but important rule in Hanafi judicial procedure: how to weigh evidence when both parties claim production of an item. The key insight is that certain acts of production, like the first shearing of wool or weaving a one-time cloth, are similar to the birth of an animal, giving the possessor's evidence priority. However, if the cause of production can recur, it is treated like ordinary ownership, and the external claimant's evidence prevails. Understanding this distinction helps clarify why some property disputes are decided based on the nature of the production process itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute type
First, determine whether the conflict is between an external claimant (kharji) and a possessor (dhu al-yad), or between two external claimants. The rule only applies in the former scenario.
If both are external, evidence is equally weighted and the item is divided.
2
Classify the cause of production
Examine the act of production claimed (e.g., weaving, shearing, building). Ask: Is this cause naturally repetitive (e.g., building, planting, shearing wool every year) or is it a one-time event (e.g., weaving a cotton cloth that cannot be rewoven, first shearing of a fleece)?
The text gives examples: cotton garments (non-repetitive) vs. silk garments (repetitive).
3
Apply the analogy to birth
If the cause is non-repetitive, the possessor's evidence is preferred because the production is akin to birth (nataj) – a one-time origin of ownership. If repetitive, the external claimant's evidence is preferred, as the item is treated like any other owned property.
The default rule favors the external claimant; the birth analogy is the exception.
4
Handle uncertainty
When it is unclear whether the cause is repetitive, consult experts (ahl al-khibra). If experts cannot determine, the external claimant wins because the original rule (preference for external evidence) remains.
This ensures that the departure from the norm is only applied when certainty exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بتنازع الخارج مع ذي اليد إذ لو كانا خارجين ادعى كل دابة في يد آخر وبرهنا على النتاج فإنهما يستويان ويقضى بها بينهما كما في كافي الحاكم. وفي شهادات البزازية : عاين الشاهد دابة تتبع دابة وترتضع له أن يشهد بالملك والنتاج اهـ. وفي الخلاصة وعلى هذا لو شهد شاهدان على النتاج لزيد وآخران على النتاج لعمرو ويتصور هذا بأن رأى الشاهدان أنه ارتضع من لبن أنثى كانت له في ملكه وآخران رأيا أنه ارتضع من لبن انثى في ملك آخر فتحل الشهادة للفريقين اهـ. وألحقوا بالنتاج ما لا يتكرر سببه لكونه في معناه لأنه دعوى أولية الملك كالنسج في الثياب التي لا تنسج إلا مرة كالثياب القطنية وغزل القطن وجلب اللبن واتخاذ الجبن واللبد والمرعزي وجز الصوف، وإن كان سبباً يتكرر لا يكون في معناه فيقضى به للخارج بمنزلة الملك المطلق مثل الجز والبناء والغرس وزراعة الحنطة والحبوب، فإن أشكل يرجع إلى أهل الخبرة، فإن أشكل عليهم قضى به للخارج لأن القضاء ببينة هو الأصل، وإنما عد لنا عنه بخبر النتاج فإذا برهن الخارج أنه ثوبه نسجه وبرهن ذو اليد كذلك، فإن علم أنه لا ينسج إلا مرة فهو لذي اليد، وإن علم تكرار نسجه فهو للخارج كالخز. وكذا إذا أشكل وكذا إذا اختلفا في صوف وبرهن كل أنه صوفه جزه من غنمه فإنه يقضى به لذي اليد وأورد كيف يكون الجز في معناه وهو ليس بسبب لأولية الملك لأن الصوف كان مملوكاً له قبله .
And it is conditioned that the dispute is between the external claimant and the possessor; if both were external claimants, each claiming an animal in the hand of another and both prove birth, then they are equal and it is judged between them as in Kafi al-Hakim. And in Shahadat al-Bazzaziyya: The witness saw an animal following another and suckling from it: he may testify to ownership and birth. In al-Khulasa: On this, if two witnesses testify to birth for Zayd and two others for Amr, and this is conceivable if the two saw it suckle from the milk of a female that was owned by Zayd, and two saw it suckle from the milk of a female in another's ownership, then testimony from both groups is valid. They extended the ruling of birth to what does not have a recurring cause, because it is similar in meaning: it is a claim of original ownership, like weaving in garments that are only woven once, like cotton garments, spinning cotton, bringing milk, making cheese, felt, woolen yarn, and shearing wool. But if the cause recurs, it is not similar, so judgment is given to the external claimant as in absolute ownership, like shearing, building, planting, cultivating wheat and grains. If it is unclear, experts are consulted. If it is unclear to them, judgment goes to the external claimant because judgment with evidence is the principle, and we deviate from it only due to the report on birth. So if the external claimant proves that his cloth was woven by him, and the possessor also proves that, then if it is known that it is only woven once, it is for the possessor; if it is known that its weaving recurs, it is for the external claimant, like silk. Likewise if it is unclear. Also if they dispute over wool and each proves that it is his wool sheared from his sheep, then judgment is for the possessor. And it is asked how shearing can be in its meaning when it is not a cause for original ownership because the wool was owned by him before?
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage first addresses cases where both disputants are external claimants (neither possesses the property). Here, their evidence of birth is equal and the property is divided between them. Then it defines which types of production are analogous to birth (nataj) for purposes of preferring the possessor's evidence. The rule is: if the cause of production is non-repetitive (i.e., the item is only produced once, like a specific woven cloth, or the first shearing of wool, or the production of cheese from milk), then it is treated like birth, and the possessor's evidence is preferred. If the cause is repetitive (like shearing wool annually, building a house, planting crops), then it is treated like absolute ownership, and the external claimant's evidence takes precedence. If it is unclear whether the cause is repetitive, experts are consulted; if they cannot determine, the external claimant wins because the default is that evidence of the external claimant is preferred unless the special status of birth applies. The passage also includes an example about wool: if each proves he sheared the wool from his own sheep, the possessor wins because shearing is analogous to birth (first time only). The question about how shearing can be in the meaning of original ownership is answered by noting that the wool was owned before shearing, but the shearing itself is a cause of possession of the fleece; however, since shearing can recur, it should not be analogous. The text seems to treat the first shearing as analogous because it establishes original ownership of the fleece at the moment of shearing.

Key Terms
نتاج (nataj): Birth of an animal; extended to non-repetitive production.
نسج (nasj): Weaving cloth; considered non-repetitive if the cloth is only woven once.
جز (jazz): Shearing wool; if the first shearing, it is analogous to birth; if repeated, it is not.
بناء (bina'): Building; a repetitive cause (one can build multiple times).
غرس (ghars): Planting; repetitive cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two parties dispute a cheese. The possessor proves he made the cheese from his own milk; the external claimant proves he made it from his milk. It is known that cheese making is typically a one-time process (milk is converted into cheese and cannot be reused). Who wins?
A) External claimant
B) Possessor
C) Both share
D) Experts decide

Key Takeaway
Non-repetitive causes of production are treated like birth, favoring the possessor; repetitive causes favor the external claimant.
